Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
10 min
When Emancipation Does Not Cut Off the Progression of an Injury
Explains when emancipation cuts off progression of injury

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on when emancipation cuts off the progression of an injury and distinguish between accidental and intentional killing in its application.

Core Idea
Emancipation cuts off sirayah only when there is doubt about the right to compensation.

This lesson explores a key principle in Hanafi fiqh: emancipation of a slave does not automatically stop the progression of an injury (sirayah). Rather, it depends on whether the injury occurs before or after freedom and whether there is confusion about who is entitled to compensation. The passage distinguishes between accidental and intentional killing and highlights an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa (with Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The text states: 'al-I'tāq lā yaqṭaʿu al-sirāyah li-dhātihī bal al-ishtibāh man lahu al-ḥaqq.' Emancipation does not cut off sirayah by itself; the cutting occurs only when there is doubt over who holds the right to compensation.
This is a crucial distinction: the act of freeing does not retroactively affect injuries.
2
Apply the principle to accidental killing
In accidental killing (khaṭaʾ), the injury occurs while the slave is still a slave, so the right to compensation belongs to the master. If the slave later dies after emancipation, the right would belong to the slave's heirs, causing doubt. Therefore, emancipation cuts off sirayah in accidental killing by consensus.
3
Distinguish intentional killing
For intentional killing (ʿamd), the consequence is qiṣāṣ (retribution). If the slave has no heir other than the master, there is no doubt about who holds the right (the master), so emancipation does not cut off sirayah according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Imam Muhammad disagrees when the slave has no other heir.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Muhammad holds that emancipation does cut off sirayah even in this case.
4
Understand the underlying reason
The doubt arises because the right to compensation shifts: at the time of injury, the master is entitled to compensation for the slave; after death, the slave's heirs are entitled to blood-money. If there is no doubt (i.e., only the master is heir), the right remains with the master, so the progression continues.
This is a qiyās-based reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في أحدهما فارشهما للسيد فقا عيني عبد دفع سيده عبده وأخذ قيمته أو أمسكه ولا وقد لا يثبت الحل أصلاً، ولأن ما ادعى كل واحد منهما من السبب للحل انتفى بانكار الآخر فبقي بلا سبب فلا يثبت الحل بدونه إذا لا يجري فيه البدل بخلاف ما نحن فيه لأن السبب موجود بيقين ولا منكر له فلم يوجد ما يبطله ولا ما يحتمل الإبطال فأمكن استيفاؤه، والإعتاق لا يقطع السراية لذاته بل الاشتباه من له الحق وذلك إذا كان له وارث غير المولى على ما بينا أو في الأطراف أو في القتل خطأ لأن العبد لا يصلح مالكاً للمال فعلى اعتبار حالة الجرح يكون الحق للمولى وعلى اعتبار حالة الموت أو زيادة الجرح في الحالة الثانية يكون للعبد حتى تقضى منه ديونه وتنفذ وصاياه فحصل الاشتباه فيمن له الحق فسقط . ما حدث بعد الحرية من ذلك الجرح. وأما القتل عمداً فموجبه القصاص فلا اشتباه فيه إذا لم يكن له وارث سوى المولى لأنه على اعتبار أن يكون الحق للعبد فالمولى هو الذي يتولاه فلا اشتباه فيمن له الحق ، فالحاصل من هذا كله أن من قطع يد عبد غيره فأعتقه المولى ثم مات لا يزيد على أربع لأنه إما إن قطع عمداً أو خطأ، فإن كان الأول فإما أن يكون للعبد وارث سوى المولى أو لم يكن فإن كان يقطع الإعتاق السراية بالاتفاق فلا يجب القصاص لجهالة المقضى له والمقضى به، وإن لم يكن لا يقطعها عندهما خلافاً لمحمد وإن كان الثاني فالإعتاق لا يقطعها فحاصله أنهم أجمعوا في الخطأ وفي العمد فيما إذا كان له وارث آخر أن الإعتاق يقطع السراية فلا يجب إلا أرش القطع وما ينقص بذلك إلا الإعتاق ويسقط الدية والقصاص، وكذا في القطع إذا لم يمت منه لا يجب عليه سوى أرش القطع وما نقص إلى الإعتاق ولا يجب عليه ما حدث من النقصان بعد الإعتاق بالإجماع فعلم بذلك أن كل موضع لا يجب فيه القصاص يجب فيه أرش القطع، وما نقصه إلى الإعتاق ولا يجب عليه الدية وما نقص منه بعد الإعتاق . قال رحمه الله : (قال أحدكما حر فشجا فبين في أحدهما فارشهما للسيد) يعني إذا قال لعبديه أحدكما حر ثم شجا فبين في أحدهما العتق بعد الشج فأرشهما للمولى لأن العتق غير نازل في المعين فالشجة تصادف المعين فبقيا مملوكين في حق الشجة ولو قتلهما رجلا واحد في وقت واحد معاً تجب دية حر وقيمة عبد والفرق أن البيان إنشاء من وجه وإظهار من وجه على ما عرف وبعد الشجة بقي محلاً للبيان فاعتبر إنشاء في حق المحل وبعد الموت لم يبق محلاً للبيان فاعتبر إظهاراً محضاً، فإذا قتلهما رجل واحد معاً فأحدهما حر يجب عليه دية حر وقيمة عبد فيكون الكل نصفين بين المولى والورثة لعدم الأولوية، وإن اختلفت قيمتهما يجب نصف قيمة كل واحد منهما ودية حر فيقسم مثل الأول بخلاف ما إذا قتلهما على التعاقب حيث تجب عليه قيمة الأول لمولاه ودية الثاني للورثة، وبخلاف ما إذا قتل كل واحد منهما رجلاً معاً تجب قيمة المملوكين لأنا لم نتيقن بقتل كل واحد منهما حراً وكل منهما ينكر ذلك، ولأن البحر الرائق ج ٩/ م ١٢ العلم القياس يأبى ثبوت العتق في المجهول لأنه لا يفيد فائدته وإنما صححناه ضرورة صحة التصرف وأثبتنا له ولاية لنقل من المجهول إلى المعلوم فيقدر بقدر الضرورة وهي النفس دون الأطراف والدية فبقي مملوكاً في حقهما فتجب القيمة فيهما فيكون نصفين بين المولى والورثة فيأخذ هو نصف كل واحد منهما ويترك النصف لورثته لأن موجب العتق ثابت في أحدهما في حق المولى فلا بدل له فوزع ذلك عليهما نصفين وإن قتلاهما على التعاقب فعلى قاتل الأول قيمته للمولى لتعينه للرق، وعلى قاتل الثاني ديته لورثته لتعينه للعتق بعد موت الأول. وإن كان لا يدري أيهما قتل أولاً فعلى كل واحد منهما قيمته وللمولى من كل واحد منهما نصف القيمة كالأول لعدم أولوية أحدهما بالتقدم.
In one of them, their blood-money is for the master... [the text continues]... and the doubt is about who has the right. And that is if he has an heir other than the master as we explained, or in limbs, or in accidental killing because the slave is not fit to own property. So considering the state of wound, the right is for the master; considering the state of death or increase of wound in the second state, it is for the slave so that his debts are paid and his bequests executed. Thus doubt arises about who has the right, so what occurs after freedom from that wound is dropped. As for intentional killing, its consequence is retribution, so no doubt when he has no heir other than the master... The conclusion of all this is that whoever cuts off the hand of another's slave, then the master frees him, then he dies, does not exceed four because either he cut intentionally or accidentally... The Imam (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'He said: One of you is free, then he caused a head wound, then he specified concerning one of them, so their blood-money is for the master.' Meaning: if he said to his two slaves, 'One of you is free', then one caused a head wound, then he specified the emancipation after the wound, so their blood-money is for the master because the emancipation is not attached to a specific one, so the wound encounters a specific one, so they both remain slaves with respect to the wound. And if a single man killed both of them at the same time, the blood-money of a free man and the value of a slave are obligatory. The difference is that specification is in a way creation and in a way manifestation as known, and after the wound it remains a locus for specification, so it is considered creation with respect to the locus; after death it is no longer a locus for specification, so it is considered pure manifestation...
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal commentary from Al-Bahr al-Raiq on the chapter of injuries involving slaves. It begins by addressing a scenario where a master says 'one of you is free' to his two slaves, then one slave commits a head wound (shajjah), and the master subsequently specifies which slave he meant. The ruling: the blood-money (arsh) for the wound goes to the master, because at the time of the wound both slaves were still slaves—the emancipation was not yet specified. The text then explores the principle that emancipation does not cut off the progression (sirayah) of an injury by itself; rather, the issue is confusion about who holds the right to compensation. If the slave has another heir besides the master, the right becomes ambiguous and the progression is cut off. The author distinguishes between intentional killing (where retribution is the consequence) and accidental killing (where blood-money applies). There is also an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa's position (with Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differing) regarding whether emancipation cuts off progression in intentional killing when the slave has no heir other than the master. The matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq is then quoted: 'He said: One of you is free, then he caused a head wound, then he specified concerning one of them, so their blood-money is for the master.' The commentary explains that the wound occurs while the slave is still a slave, so the master gets the compensation. The passage ends with scenarios where a man kills both slaves (one actually free) simultaneously or successively, and how the blood-money and value are divided between the master and the heirs of the free slave, applying the principle of qiyas that ambiguous emancipation does not benefit for limbs and blood-money.

Key Terms
السراية (al-sirāyah): Progression of an injury from a wound to death or further harm.
الإعتاق (al-i'tāq): Manumission; the act of freeing a slave.
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retribution; judicial punishment equivalent to the crime (eye for eye).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In accidental killing, does emancipation cut off sirayah according to all Hanafi imams?
A) Yes, it cuts off by consensus
B) No, it never cuts off
C) Only if the slave has another heir
D) It cuts off only according to Muhammad

Key Takeaway
Emancipation cuts off the progression of an injury only when there is doubt about who holds the right to compensation, not automatically.

Diyat — Blood Money

Ambiguous Emancipation and Injury: Compensation to the Master
Eplains ruling of ambiguous emancipation with head wound

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a master says 'one of you is free' and a slave commits a head wound, and explain why the compensation goes to the master.

Core Idea
Wound before specification: both slaves remain slaves, so compensation goes to the master.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from Kanz al-Daqaiq: a master declares 'one of you is free' to his two slaves, then one slave commits a head wound (shajjah), and the master subsequently specifies which slave he meant. The commentary explains that the compensation (arsh) goes to the master because at the time of the wound, both slaves were still slaves—the emancipation was not yet attached to a specific individual. The passage also extends to cases where both slaves are killed simultaneously or successively by a third party, and how blood-money and value are distributed between the master and the heirs of the freed slave.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
A master says to his two slaves: 'One of you is free.' Then one of them commits a head wound (shajjah) on a third party. After the wound, the master specifies that the slave who committed the wound is the one he meant to free.
The specification occurs after the wound.
2
Identify the ruling
The compensation (arsh) for the wound is for the master, not for the slave or the victim. Because at the time of the wound, both slaves were still slaves (the emancipation was not yet specified), so the master is entitled to the compensation for the injury caused by his slave.
3
Distinguish between person and limbs
The text explains that ambiguous emancipation is valid for the person (the slave becomes free) but not for limbs or blood-money. The qiyas (analogy) would reject establishing freedom in an ambiguous context, but it is validated by necessity (darurah) for transactions, limited to the person only.
This is the key principle: the emancipation is effective for the person but not for the financial consequences of injuries.
4
Apply to simultaneous killing
If a third party kills both slaves at the same time, one of whom is actually free (the specified one), the killer must pay the blood-money of a free person (diya) and the value of a slave. Since it is unknown which is free, the total sum is divided equally between the master (who gets the slave's value) and the heirs of the free slave (who get the diya).
Specification after death is considered pure manifestation (izhar), not creation (insha).
5
Apply to successive killing
If the two slaves are killed one after the other, the killer of the first pays the slave's value to the master (because the first was still a slave at death), and the killer of the second pays the diya of a free person to the heirs (because after the first died, the second became the specific free slave).

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal commentary from Al-Bahr al-Raiq on the chapter of injuries involving slaves. It begins by addressing a scenario where a master says 'one of you is free' to his two slaves, then one slave commits a head wound (shajjah), and the master subsequently specifies which slave he meant. The ruling: the blood-money (arsh) for the wound goes to the master, because at the time of the wound both slaves were still slaves—the emancipation was not yet specified. The text then explores the principle that emancipation does not cut off the progression (sirayah) of an injury by itself; rather, the issue is confusion about who holds the right to compensation. If the slave has another heir besides the master, the right becomes ambiguous and the progression is cut off. The author distinguishes between intentional killing (where retribution is the consequence) and accidental killing (where blood-money applies). There is also an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa's position (with Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differing) regarding whether emancipation cuts off progression in intentional killing when the slave has no heir other than the master. The matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq is then quoted: 'He said: One of you is free, then he caused a head wound, then he specified concerning one of them, so their blood-money is for the master.' The commentary explains that the wound occurs while the slave is still a slave, so the master gets the compensation. The passage ends with scenarios where a man kills both slaves (one actually free) simultaneously or successively, and how the blood-money and value are divided between the master and the heirs of the free slave, applying the principle of qiyas that ambiguous emancipation does not benefit for limbs and blood-money.

Key Terms
الإعتاق المبهم (al-i'tāq al-mubham): Emancipation that is ambiguous, i.e., not assigned to a specific slave.
الشجة (al-shajjah): A head wound that reaches the bone.
البيان (al-bayān): Specification; the act of clarifying which slave is intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a third party kills both slaves simultaneously after the master says 'one of you is free' but before specification, how is the compensation distributed?
A) Full blood-money for a free person goes to the heirs, full slave value goes to the master
B) The total (diya of free + value of slave) is divided equally between master and heirs
C) Each killer pays half to master and half to heirs
D) The master gets nothing because the slaves are now free

Key Takeaway
When a master ambiguously frees one of two slaves, then a wound occurs before specification, the compensation goes to the master because both slaves are still property at that time.

The Master's Choice When an Ambiguously Emancipated Slave Commits Manslaughter
Rulings on ambiguous emancipation and manslaughter from Al-Jami' al-Saghir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a master ambiguously emancipates one of two slaves and one slave commits manslaughter, and identify the options depending on whom the master specifies.

Core Idea
Specifying the offender slave constitutes choosing ransom; specifying the non-offender preserves the master's choice.

In this passage from Al-Jami' al-Saghir, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani presents a scenario where a master says 'one of you is free' to two slaves, and then one kills a man accidentally. The judge compels specification, and the ruling changes based on whom the master frees. This lesson unpacks the core ruling and contrasts it with a sale with option case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial scenario
The master has two slaves and says in his health 'one of you is free'. Then one slave commits accidental killing (manslaughter).
The emancipation is ambiguous, so the identity of the freed slave is unknown.
2
Note the judge's intervention
The judge forces the master to specify which slave he meant to free. This is compulsory (yujbir).
The master cannot remain ambiguous after the crime.
3
Track the two outcomes based on specification
If the master specifies the non-offender, he retains the choice (khiyar) to either hand over the offender slave (daf') or ransom him (fida') by paying his value. If he specifies the offender, he is deemed to have chosen ransom (mukhtar li al-fida).
The second outcome is not a choice; it is a legal consequence.
4
Contrast with the sale with option case
In a sale with a three-day option, if the slave commits a crime in the seller's hand and the seller ratifies the sale knowing the crime, he does not become a chooser of ransom even though he disables himself from handing over. Similarly for the buyer who returns the slave after a crime.
The key distinction: in the sale case, the act of ratification or return is not considered choosing ransom, whereas in the emancipation case, specifying the offender is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الجامع الصغير : وإذا قال الرجل لعبدين له في صحته أحدكما حر ثم إن أحدهما قتل رجلاً خطأ فالقاضي يجبر المولى على البيان، فإن أوقع العتق على غير الجاني خير في الثاني بين الدفع والفداء، وإن أوقع العتق على الجاني صار مختاراً للفداء في الجاني فرق بين هذا وبين ما إذا باع عبداً على أنه بالخيار ثلاثة أيام فجنى العبد في يد البائع جناية موجبة للمولى في مدة الخيار بأن قتل رجلاً خطأ فأجاز البائع البيع فيه مع العلم بالجناية لم يصر مختاراً للفداء، وإن أعجز نفسه عن الدفع مع بالجناية. وكذا إذا كان الخيار للمشتري فجنى العبد في مدة الخيار ثم رد المشتري العبد لا يكون مختاراً للفداء، وإن عجز نفسه عن الدفع بسبب الرد بالجناية. ولو كان كل واحد من العبدين قتل رجلاً خطأ بعد العتق المبهم ثم أوقع المولى العتق على أحدهما بعينه يخير بين الدفع والفداء في العبد الأخير وعليه قيمة العبد الذي أوقع فيه العتق لولي الجناية يريد إذا كانت قيمته أقل من الدية ولم يصر مختاراً للفداء بصرف العتق إلى الجاني، فرق بين هذا وبين ما لو طلق إحدى امرأتيه في صحته ثلاثاً ثم مرض مرض الموت فأجبر على البيان فأوقع ذلك على أحدهما فإنه يصير فاراً وإن كان مضطراً إلى البيان، وكذلك لو كانت جناية أحد العبدين قطع يد وجناية الآخر قتل نفس خطأ كان الجواب كما قلنا ولو قال في صحته لعبدين قيمة كل واحد منهما ألف أحدكما حر . ثم قتل أحدهما رجلاً خطأ ثم مات المولى قبل البيان عتق من كل واحد منهما نصفه وسعى كل واحد منهما في نصف قيمته وللمجني عليه في مال المولى قيمة الجاني يريد به إذا كانت قيمته أقل من الأرش ويصير من جميع ماله ولا يصير المولى مختاراً للفداء. ولو كان كل واحد من العبدين قتل رجلاً خطأ والمسألة بحالها سعى كل واحد من العبدين في نصف قيمته ولكل واحد من المجني عليهما في مال المولى قيمة العبد الذي جنى عليه ولم يصر المولى مختاراً للفداء هذا الذي ذكرناه كله إذا أوقع المولى العتق المبهم على أحد عبديه قبل الجناية، وأما إذا كان إيقاع العتق المبهم بعد الجناية فقال : رجل له عبدان قيمة كل واحد منهما ألف فقتل أحدهما قتيلاً خطأ ثم قال المولى في صحته أحدكما حر وهو عالم بالجناية ثم مات المولى قبل البيان عتق من كل واحد منهما نصفه وسعى كل واحد منهما في
And in Al-Jami' al-Saghir: When a man says to his two slaves in his health, 'One of you is free,' then one of them kills a man accidentally, the judge forces the master to specify. If he places the emancipation on the non-offender, he is given a choice in the second between handing over and ransoming. If he places the emancipation on the offender, he becomes one who has chosen ransom for the offender. There is a distinction between this and when he sells a slave with a three-day option, and the slave commits a crime in the seller's hand that obligates the master during the option period by killing a man accidentally, and the seller ratifies the sale with knowledge of the crime—he does not become a chooser of ransom, even though he incapacitates himself from handing over due to the crime. Likewise, if the option is for the buyer and the slave commits a crime during the option period, then the buyer returns the slave, he is not considered a chooser of ransom, even though he incapacitates himself from handing over by returning due to the crime. And if each of the two slaves killed a man accidentally after the ambiguous emancipation, then the master places the emancipation on one specifically, he is given a choice between handing over and ransoming for the last slave, and upon him is the value of the slave on whom he placed the emancipation for the guardian of the crime, meaning if his value is less than the blood-money, and he does not become a chooser of ransom by directing the emancipation to the offender. There is a distinction between this and if he divorced one of his wives three times in his health, then became sick with a mortal illness and was forced to specify, and he placed that on one of them—he becomes a defrauder (farr) even though he was compelled to specify. Similarly, if the crime of one slave was cutting off a hand and the crime of the other was accidental killing of a life, the answer is as we said. And if he said in his health to two slaves, each worth one thousand, 'One of you is free,' then one of them killed a man accidentally, then the master died before specification, half of each slave becomes free, and each slave works for half his value, and for the victim is the value of the offender from the master's estate, meaning if his value is less than the compensation, and it becomes from all his property, and the master does not become a chooser of ransom. And if each of the two slaves killed a man accidentally and the case is the same, each slave works for half his value, and for each victim is the value of the slave who committed the crime from the master's estate, and the master does not become a chooser of ransom. All this is if the master placed the ambiguous emancipation on one of his slaves before the crime. But if the placement of ambiguous emancipation was after the crime—he said: A man has two slaves each worth one thousand; one killed a man accidentally, then the master said in his health, 'One of you is free,' while knowing the crime, then the master died before specification—half of each slave becomes free, and each slave works for half his value...
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from Al-Jami' al-Saghir by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. It deals with scenarios where a master ambiguously emancipates one of two slaves ('one of you is free') and then a slave commits manslaughter (accidental killing). The judge compels the master to specify which slave he meant. If the master specifies the non-offender, he has a choice regarding the offender slave: either hand him over to the victim's family (who may take him as compensation) or ransom him by paying his value. If he specifies the offender, he is considered to have chosen ransom, and must pay the value. The text distinguishes this from a sale with option: if a slave under option commits a crime, the seller or buyer does not become a chooser of ransom even if they disable themselves from handing over. There is also a distinction from a case of divorce where the divorcer is considered a defrauder (firar) to avoid inheritance. The passage then addresses scenarios where both slaves commit manslaughter, or where the master dies before specification, leading to half-freedom and working for the value. It concludes by noting that the ruling differs depending on whether the ambiguous emancipation was before or after the crime. The window ends mid-sentence, but the pattern is clear: when the master places emancipation after the crime with knowledge, and dies before specification, half of each slave becomes free and they work for half their value.

Key Terms
الدفع (al-daf'): Handing over the slave to the victim's family as compensation for the killing.
الفداء (al-fidā'): Ransoming the slave by paying his value to the victim's family.
خيار الفداء (khiyār al-fidā'): The master's option to either hand over the slave or ransom him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Jami' al-Saghir, if a master says 'one of you is free' and a slave commits manslaughter, and the master specifies the offender, what is the consequence?
A) He must hand over the offender.
B) He is given a choice between handing over and ransoming.
C) He becomes a chooser of ransom and must pay the slave's value.
D) He must pay full blood-money.

Key Takeaway
When a master ambiguously frees one of two slaves and a slave kills accidentally, specifying the offender obligates ransom, while specifying the non-offender preserves the master's choice between daf' and fida'.

Further Scenarios: Both Slaves Kill, Death Before Specification, and Emancipation After Crime
Further rulings on ambiguous emancipation and manslaughter from Al-Jami' al-Saghir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rulings when both slaves commit manslaughter after ambiguous emancipation, when the master dies before specification, and when emancipation is declared after the crime with knowledge.

Core Idea
In complex scenarios, the master's specification triggers different obligations, and death before specification leads to half-freedom and working for value.

This passage continues from Al-Jami' al-Saghir with more intricate cases. When both slaves kill, when the master dies before specifying, and when the master declares emancipation after the crime knowing of it, the rulings adapt. We will explore these additional cases and the distinctions that govern them.

Guided Path
1
Understand the both-slaves-kill scenario
Both slaves kill accidentally after ambiguous emancipation. The master specifies one. He is given a choice between daf' and fida' for the other slave (the one not specified), and he must pay the value of the specified slave to the guardian of the crime if it is less than diyah. He does NOT become a chooser of ransom by specifying the offender.
This is different from the single-crime case where specifying the offender makes him a chooser of ransom.
2
Understand the death before specification scenario
The master says 'one of you is free', then one slave kills, then the master dies before specifying. Half of each slave becomes free, they must work for half their value to pay their own ransom, and the victim gets the value of the offender from the master's estate (if less than diyah). The master is not considered a chooser of ransom.
The master's death crystallizes the ambiguous emancipation into half-freedom for each.
3
Understand the emancipation after crime scenario
If the master says 'one of you is free' after the crime, with knowledge of it, and dies before specification, the same ruling applies: half free, work for value, and victim gets value from estate. No chooser of ransom.
The knowledge of the crime does not change the outcome; the master still does not become a chooser of ransom.
4
Note the distinction from divorce
In the divorce case, if a man ambiguously divorces one wife, then falls terminally ill and is forced to specify, specifying one wife makes him a defrauder (farr) to avoid inheritance—even though he was compelled. In emancipation, specifying the offender does not make him a defrauder.
This shows that similar actions can have different legal presumptions based on context.

الفداء (al-fidā'): Ransoming the slave by paying his value to the victim's family.
الفرار (al-firār): Fraudulent evasion of inheritance by divorcing a wife during mortal illness.

In the both-slaves-kill scenario of ambiguous emancipation, what happens when the master specifies one slave?
A) He becomes a chooser of ransom for that slave.

When both slaves kill after ambiguous emancipation, specifying one slave gives the master a choice for the other and requires payment of the specified slave's value; if the master dies before specification, each slave becomes half-free and works for value.

Foundational Ruling: Validity of Ambiguous Manumission by Istihsan
Explains the Hanafi validation of ambiguous manumission by istihsan and its limited effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that ambiguous manumission is valid despite qiyas and explain why its effect is limited to the slave's person, not limbs or blood-money.

Core Idea
Ambiguous manumission is validated by necessity, but its effect is limited to freeing the person, not the limbs or diya.

The Hanafi school validates a declaration like 'one of you is free' even though the slave is not identified. This is a departure from strict qiyas, permitted by istihsan to facilitate transactions. However, because the manumission is inherently uncertain, its legal effect is confined to what is necessary: the slave's life (person), not limbs or financial liability. This lesson unpacks that foundational principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key act and its ruling
The act is the master saying to a group of slaves 'one of you is free' without specifying. State that the Hanafi school rules this as valid (sahih).
2
Understand the qiyas objection
Qiyas would reject this because manumission of an unknown person does not confer the full benefit of freedom. Recognize that this is the starting point for the Hanafi discussion.
3
Apply istihsan to override qiyas
The Hanafi school validates it by istihsan (juristic preference) due to the necessity of validating transactions. Note that this is a different form of reasoning than qiyas.
Istihsan is used here as an exceptional proof.
4
Limit the effect of the manumission
The effect of ambiguous manumission is limited to the slave's person (nafs), not limbs or money (diya). So the slave remains a slave regarding limbs and diya.
This limitation is crucial for subsequent tort cases.
5
Note the consequence for tort liability
Because the slave is partly free (person) and partly slave (limbs/diya), when a tort occurs, the value is split between the master and the slave's heirs.
This sets the stage for the detailed cases in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات باب جناية المملوك والجناية عليه
العلم
القياس يأبى ثبوت العتق في المجهول لأنه لا يفيد فائدته وإنما صححناه ضرورة صحة التصرف وأثبتنا له ولاية لنقل من المجهول إلى المعلوم فيقدر بقدر الضرورة وهي النفس دون الأطراف والدية فبقي مملوكاً في حقهما فتجب القيمة فيهما فيكون نصفين بين المولى والورثة فيأخذ هو نصف كل واحد منهما ويترك النصف لورثته لأن موجب العتق ثابت في أحدهما في حق المولى فلا بدل له فوزع ذلك عليهما نصفين وإن قتلاهما على التعاقب فعلى قاتل الأول قيمته للمولى لتعينه للرق، وعلى قاتل الثاني ديته لورثته لتعينه للعتق بعد موت الأول. وإن كان لا يدري أيهما قتل أولاً فعلى كل واحد منهما قيمته وللمولى من كل واحد منهما نصف القيمة كالأول لعدم أولوية أحدهما بالتقدم. وفي الجامع الصغير : وإذا قال الرجل لعبدين له في صحته أحدكما حر ثم إن أحدهما قتل رجلاً خطأ فالقاضي يجبر المولى على البيان، فإن أوقع العتق على غير الجاني خير في الثاني بين الدفع والفداء، وإن أوقع العتق على الجاني صار مختاراً للفداء في الجاني فرق بين هذا وبين ما إذا باع عبداً على أنه بالخيار ثلاثة أيام فجنى العبد في يد البائع جناية موجبة للمولى في مدة الخيار بأن قتل رجلاً خطأ فأجاز البائع البيع فيه مع العلم بالجناية لم يصر مختاراً للفداء، وإن أعجز نفسه عن الدفع مع الجناية. وكذا إذا كان الخيار للمشتري فجنى العبد في مدة الخيار ثم رد المشتري العبد لا يكون مختاراً للفداء وإن عجز نفسه عن الدفع بسبب الرد بالجناية. ولو كان كل واحد من العبدين قتل رجلاً خطأ بعد العتق المبهم ثم أوقع المولى العتق على أحدهما بعينه يخير بين الدفع والفداء في العبد الأخير وعليه قيمة العبد الذي أوقع فيه العتق لولي الجناية يريد إذا كانت قيمته أقل من الدية ولم يصر مختاراً للفداء بصرف العتق إلى الجاني، فرق بين هذا وبين ما لو طلق إحدى امرأتيه في صحته ثلاثاً ثم مرض مرض الموت فأجبر على البيان فأوقع ذلك على أحدهما فإنه يصير فاراً وإن كان مضطراً إلى البيان، وكذلك لو كانت جناية أحد العبدين قطع يد وجناية الآخر قتل نفس خطأ كان الجواب كما قلنا ولو قال في صحته لعبدين قيمة كل واحد منهما ألف أحدكما حر ثم قتل أحدهما رجلاً خطأ ثم مات المولى قبل البيان عتق من كل واحد منهما نصفه وسعى كل واحد منهما في نصف قيمته وللمجني عليه في مال المولى قيمة الجاني يريد به إذا كانت قيمته أقل من الأرش ويصير من جميع ماله ولا يصير المولى مختاراً للفداء. ولو كان كل واحد من العبدين قتل رجلاً خطأ والمسألة بحالها سعى كل واحد من العبدين في نصف قيمته ولكل واحد من المجني عليهما في مال المولى قيمة العبد الذي جنى عليه ولم يصر المولى مختاراً للفداء هذا الذي ذكرناه كله إذا أوقع المولى العتق المبهم على أحد عبديه قبل الجناية، وأما إذا كان إيقاع العتق المبهم بعد الجناية فقال : رجل له عبدان قيمة كل واحد منهما ألف فقتل أحدهما قتيلاً خطأ ثم قال المولى في صحته أحدكما حر وهو عالم بالجناية ثم مات المولى قبل البيان عتق من كل واحد منهما نصفه وسعى كل واحد منهما في نصف قيمته ويصير المولى مختاراً للفداء في الجاني، ثم إذا صار مختاراً للفداء فمقدار القيمة المال، وإذا جنى كل واحد من العبدين جناية والمسألة بحالها سعياً على الوجه الذي وصفناه وصار مختاراً للفداء في الجنايتين ولكن تجب دية واحد في مال المولى وقيمة العبدين ويكون ذلك من جميع المال وما زاد على القيمة إلى تمام الدية يعتبر من ثلث المال وتكون الجنايتان نصفين إذ ليس أحدهما أولى من الآخر. قال في الجامع الصغير : رجل له عبدان سالم ورابع فقتل سالم رجلاً خطأ في صحة المولى فقال المولى أحدكما حر ثم قتل رابع رجلاً آخر في صحة المولى ثم مات المولى قبل البيان عتق من كل واحد منهما نصفه وسعى كل واحد منهما في نصف قيمته ولزم المولى الفداء في قتل سالم وهذا منه اختيار للفداء إلا أن فداء سالم في الدية يعتبر من جميع المال وما زاد على ذلك إلى تمام الدية يعتبر من الثلث ولا يلزمه الفداء في قتل رابع ولو أن المولى لم يقل ما ذكر ولكن المولى أوقع العتق على سالم صار مختاراً للفداء في قتل سالم، وإن أوقع المولى العتق على رابع لم يصر مختاراً.
Book of Blood-Money, Chapter on Tort of the Slave and Tort Against Him. Knowledge: Qiyas rejects the establishment of manumission in the ambiguous because it does not achieve its benefit, but we have validated it due to the necessity of validating the transaction. And we established for it authority to transfer from the ambiguous to the known, so it is limited to the extent of necessity, which is the person (life), not the limbs or the blood-money. So he remains a slave regarding them, so value is obligatory for them, so it is in two halves between the master and the heirs. He (the master) takes half of each of them and leaves the half to his heirs, because the effect of manumission is established in one of them regarding the master, so he has no substitute, so that is distributed over them in halves. And if they both kill him sequentially, then upon the killer of the first is his value for the master, because he was determined for slavery, and upon the killer of the second is his blood-money for his heirs, because he was determined for manumission after the death of the first. And if it is not known which one killed first, then upon each of them is his value, and for the master from each of them half the value, as in the first case, because neither has priority over the other in precedence. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If a man says to two slaves of his in his health 'one of you is free', then one of them kills a man by mistake, the judge compels the master to clarify. Then if he applies the manumission to the non-criminal, he gives the other a choice between handing over and ransom. And if he applies the manumission to the criminal, he becomes one who has chosen the ransom for the criminal. There is a difference between this and the case where he sold a slave with the option of three days, and the slave commits a tort in the hand of the seller that obligates the master during the option period, such as killing a man by mistake, and the seller completes the sale with knowledge of the tort: he does not become one who has chosen the ransom, even though he has disabled himself from handing over due to the tort. Similarly, if the option is for the buyer and the slave commits a tort during the option period, then the buyer returns the slave, he does not become one who has chosen the ransom, even though he has disabled himself from handing over due to the return because of the tort. And if each of the two slaves killed a man by mistake after the ambiguous manumission, then the master applies the manumission to one of them specifically, he is given a choice between handing over and ransom regarding the latter slave, and he owes the value of the slave on whom he applied the manumission to the guardian of the tort, meaning if his value is less than the blood-money, and he does not become one who has chosen the ransom by directing the manumission to the criminal. There is a difference between this and the case where he divorces one of his two wives three times in his health, then falls ill with a mortal illness and is compelled to clarify, and he applies that to one of them: then he becomes one who is fleeing (to avoid inheritance), even though he was forced to clarify. And likewise, if the tort of one of the two slaves is cutting off a hand and the tort of the other is killing a person by mistake, the answer is as we said. And if he says in his health to two slaves each worth one thousand 'one of you is free', then one of them kills a man by mistake, then the master dies before clarification: half of each of them becomes free, and each of them works for half of his value, and the victim's guardian has in the master's estate the value of the criminal, meaning if his value is less than the compensation, and it is from all of his property, and the master does not become one who has chosen the ransom. And if each of the two slaves kills a man by mistake, and the case is as before, each of the two slaves works for half of his value, and each of the two victims' guardians has in the master's estate the value of the slave who committed the tort against him, and the master does not become one who has chosen the ransom. All this that we have mentioned is when the master applies the ambiguous manumission to one of his two slaves before the tort. As for when the application of the ambiguous manumission is after the tort: he said: A man has two slaves each worth one thousand, and one of them kills a man by mistake, then the master says in his health 'one of you is free' while knowing of the tort, then the master dies before clarification: half of each of them becomes free, and each of them works for half of his value, and the master becomes one who has chosen the ransom regarding the criminal. Then, when he becomes one who has chosen the ransom, the amount of the value is (paid from) the property. And if each of the two slaves commits a tort, and the case is as before, they work in the manner we described, and he becomes one who has chosen the ransom for both torts, but only one blood-money is obligatory in the master's estate and the value of the two slaves, and that is from all of the property. And what exceeds the value up to the full blood-money is considered from one-third of the property. And the two torts are halves because one is not prior to the other. He said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: A man has two slaves, Salim and Rabi'. Salim kills a man by mistake during the master's health, and the master says 'one of you is free', then Rabi' kills another man during the master's health, then the master dies before clarification: half of each of them becomes free, each of them works for half of his value, and the master is obliged to ransom for the killing of Salim. This is from him a choice for ransom, except that the ransom for Salim in the blood-money is considered from all of the property, and what exceeds that up to the full blood-money is considered from one-third, and he is not obliged to ransom for the killing of Rabi'. And if the master did not say what was mentioned, but the master applies the manumission to Salim, he becomes one who has chosen the ransom for the killing of Salim; and if he applies the manumission to Rabi', he does not become one who has chosen.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a detailed expansion on the rulings concerning ambiguous manumission (al-'itq al-mubham) when a slave commits a tort. It begins by noting that qiyas (analogy) would reject the validity of ambiguous manumission because it does not achieve the full benefit of manumission, but the Hanafis validate it out of necessity for the validity of transactions. The effect of such manumission is limited to what is necessary, which is the life of the slave (his person) but not his limbs or blood-money. The text then presents numerous cases from al-Jami' al-Saghir, each with subtle distinctions. The key principle is that when a master says 'one of you is free' to his slaves, and then a tort occurs, the master's choices and liabilities depend on timing (before or after the tort), whether the master specifies the freed slave, and whether the master dies before clarification. The passage also distinguishes this from analogous cases in sale with option and divorce to show that the master does not automatically become a 'chooser of ransom' (mukhtar li al-fida') in all situations. The core idea is that the master's ambiguous manumission is treated as a transfer from the unknown to the known, limited by necessity, and the liabilities are distributed accordingly.

Key Terms
العتق المبهم (al-'itq al-mubham): Manumission where the master says 'one of you is free' without specifying which slave among a group.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference used to override qiyas for the sake of necessity or public good.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school validate ambiguous manumission despite qiyas opposing it?
A) Because qiyas is never used in manumission cases.
B) Because of istihsan for the necessity of validating transactions.
C) Because ambiguous manumission is explicitly mentioned in the Quran.
D) Because the slave can choose to be free.

Key Takeaway
Ambiguous manumission is valid by istihsan, but its effect is limited to the slave's person, not limbs or diya.

Diyat — Blood Money
intermediate
10 min
Master's Options When Tort Occurs Before Specification
Presents the core scenario from al-Jami' al-Saghir on a tort after ambiguous manumission and the master's options.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules for a master's options (hand over or ransom) when a slave commits a tort after ambiguous manumission, and distinguish the case from sale with option and divorce.

Core Idea
If tort occurs before specification, the master is compelled to clarify; if he frees the non-criminal, he retains options; if he frees the criminal, he becomes liable for ransom.

When a tort happens after an ambiguous manumission, the master must specify which slave is free. His choice affects his liability. If he frees the non-criminal, he can choose to hand over the guilty slave or pay ransom. If he frees the guilty one, he automatically becomes liable to pay ransom (mukhtar li al-fida). This lesson examines these options and contrasts them with analogous situations in sale and divorce to highlight the unique logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the timing: tort before specification
The master said 'one of you is free' while healthy, then a tort occurs. The judge compels him to clarify which slave he meant.
This is the default scenario from al-Jami' al-Saghir.
2
Analyze the outcome if the master frees the non-criminal
If he applies manumission to the slave who did not kill, he has a choice: either hand over the guilty slave or pay ransom (fida) to keep him.
3
Analyze the outcome if the master frees the criminal
If he applies manumission to the killer slave, he becomes mukhtar li al-fida (chooser of ransom), meaning he must pay the slave's value as ransom.
He cannot hand over the slave because the slave is now free.
4
Compare with sale with option
In a sale with option, if the slave commits a tort during the option period and the seller completes the sale, he does NOT become mukhtar li al-fida, even though he can no longer hand over the slave.
The key difference: in sale, the master was not obligated to clarify; in manumission, he was compelled.
5
Contrast with divorce in mortal illness
If a man divorces one of two wives three times while healthy, then becomes ill and is forced to specify, he becomes a 'farr' (fleeing from inheritance) even though forced. This shows that compulsion does not always negate the legal consequence.
The distinction is that in divorce, the act is seen as deliberate evasion, while in manumission the master is not evading liability.

العلم
القياس يأبى ثبوت العتق في المجهول لأنه لا يفيد فائدته وإنما صححناه ضرورة صحة التصرف وأثبتنا له ولاية لنقل من المجهول إلى المعلوم فيقدر بقدر الضرورة وهي النفس دون الأطراف والدية فبقي مملوكاً في حقهما فتجب القيمة فيهما فيكون نصفين بين المولى والورثة فيأخذ هو نصف كل واحد منهما ويترك النصف لورثته لأن موجب العتق ثابت في أحدهما في حق المولى فلا بدل له فوزع ذلك عليهما نصفين وإن قتلاهما على التعاقب فعلى قاتل الأول قيمته للمولى لتعينه للرق، وعلى قاتل الثاني ديته لورثته لتعينه للعتق بعد موت الأول. وإن كان لا يدري أيهما قتل أولاً فعلى كل واحد منهما قيمته وللمولى من كل واحد منهما نصف القيمة كالأول لعدم أولوية أحدهما بالتقدم. وفي الجامع الصغير : وإذا قال الرجل لعبدين له في صحته أحدكما حر ثم إن أحدهما قتل رجلاً خطأ فالقاضي يجبر المولى على البيان، فإن أوقع العتق على غير الجاني خير في الثاني بين الدفع والفداء، وإن أوقع العتق على الجاني صار مختاراً للفداء في الجاني فرق بين هذا وبين ما إذا باع عبداً على أنه بالخيار ثلاثة أيام فجنى العبد في يد البائع جناية موجبة للمولى في مدة الخيار بأن قتل رجلاً خطأ فأجاز البائع البيع فيه مع العلم بالجناية لم يصر مختاراً للفداء، وإن أعجز نفسه عن الدفع مع الجناية. وكذا إذا كان الخيار للمشتري فجنى العبد في مدة الخيار ثم رد المشتري العبد لا يكون مختاراً للفداء وإن عجز نفسه عن الدفع بسبب الرد بالجناية. ولو كان كل واحد من العبدين قتل رجلاً خطأ بعد العتق المبهم ثم أوقع المولى العتق على أحدهما بعينه يخير بين الدفع والفداء في العبد الأخير وعليه قيمة العبد الذي أوقع على أحدهما فإنه يصير فاراً وإن كان مضطراً إلى البيان، وكذلك لو كانت جناية أحد العبدين قطع يد وجناية الآخر قتل نفس خطأ كان الجواب كما قلنا ولو قال في صحته لعبدين قيمة كل واحد منهما ألف أحدكما حر ثم قتل أحدهما رجلاً خطأ ثم مات المولى قبل البيان عتق من كل واحد منهما نصفه وسعى كل واحد منهما في نصف قيمته وللمجني عليه في مال المولى قيمة الجاني يريد به إذا كانت قيمته أقل من الأرش ويصير من جميع ماله ولا يصير المولى مختاراً للفداء. ولو كان كل واحد من العبدين قتل رجلاً خطأ والمسألة بحالها سعى كل واحد من العبدين في نصف قيمته ولكل واحد من المجني عليهما في مال المولى قيمة العبد الذي جنى عليه ولم يصر المولى مختاراً للفداء هذا الذي ذكرناه كله إذا أوقع المولى العتق المبهم على أحد عبديه قبل الجناية، وأما إذا كان إيقاع العتق المبهم بعد الجناية فقال : رجل له عبدان قيمة كل واحد منهما ألف فقتل أحدهما قتيلاً خطأ ثم قال المولى في صحته أحدكما حر وهو عالم بالجناية ثم مات المولى قبل البيان عتق من كل واحد منهما نصفه وسعى كل واحد منهما في نصف قيمته ويصير المولى مختاراً للفداء في الجاني، ثم إذا صار ذلك من جميع المال وما زاد على القيمة إلى تمام الدية يعتبر من ثلث المال وتكون الجنايتان نصفين إذ ليس أحدهما أولى من الآخر. قال في الجامع الصغير : رجل له عبدان سالم ورابع فقتل سالم رجلاً خطأ في صحة المولى فقال المولى أحدكما حر ثم قتل رابع رجلاً آخر في صحة المولى ثم مات المولى قبل البيان عتق من كل واحد منهما نصفه وسعى كل واحد منهما في نصف قيمته ولزم المولى الفداء في قتل سالم وهذا منه اختيار للفداء إلا أن فداء سالم في الدية يعتبر من جميع المال وما زاد على ذلك إلى تمام الدية يعتبر من الثلث ولا يلزمه الفداء في قتل رابع ولو أن المولى لم يقل ما ذكر ولكن المولى أوقع العتق على سالم صار مختاراً للفداء في قتل سالم، وإن أوقع المولى العتق على رابع لم يصر مختاراً.
Book of Blood-Money, Chapter on Tort of the Slave and Tort Against Him. Knowledge: Qiyas rejects the establishment of manumission in the ambiguous because it does not achieve its benefit, but we have validated it due to the necessity of validating the transaction. And we established for it authority to transfer from the ambiguous to the known, so it is limited to the extent of necessity, which is the person (life), not the limbs or the blood-money. So he remains a slave regarding them, so value is obligatory for them, so it is in two halves between the master and the heirs. He (the master) takes half of each of them and leaves the half to his heirs, because the effect of manumission is established in one of them regarding the master, so he has no substitute, so that is distributed over them in halves. And if they both kill him sequentially, then upon the killer of the first is his value for the master, because he was determined for slavery, and upon the killer of the second is his blood-money for his heirs, because he was determined for manumission after the death of the first. And if it is not known which one killed first, then upon each of them is his value, and for the master from each of them half the value, as in the first case, because neither has priority over the other in precedence. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If a man says to two slaves of his in his health 'one of you is free', then one of them kills a man by mistake, the judge compels the master to clarify. Then if he applies the manumission to the non-criminal, he gives the other a choice between handing over and ransom. And if he applies the manumission to the criminal, he becomes one who has chosen the ransom for the criminal. There is a difference between this and the case where he sold a slave with the option of three days, and the slave commits a tort in the hand of the seller that obligates the master during the option period, such as killing a man by mistake, and the seller completes the sale with knowledge of the tort: he does not become one who has chosen the ransom, even though he has disabled himself from handing over due to the tort. Similarly, if the option is for the buyer and the slave commits a tort during the option period, then the buyer returns the slave, he does not become one who has chosen the ransom, even though he has disabled himself from handing over due to the return because of the tort. And if each of the two slaves killed a man by mistake after the ambiguous manumission, then the master applies the manumission to one of them specifically, he is given a choice between handing over and ransom regarding the latter slave, and he owes the value of the slave on whom he applied the manumission to the guardian of the tort, meaning if his value is less than the blood-money, and he does not become one who has chosen the ransom by directing the manumission to the criminal. There is a difference between this and the case where he divorces one of his two wives three times in his health, then falls ill with a mortal illness and is compelled to clarify, and he applies that to one of them: then he becomes one who is fleeing (to avoid inheritance), even though he was forced to clarify. And likewise, if the tort of one of the two slaves is cutting off a hand and the tort of the other is killing a person by mistake, the answer is as we said. And if he says in his health to two slaves each worth one thousand 'one of you is free', then one of them kills a man by mistake, then the master dies before clarification: half of each of them becomes free, and each of them works for half of his value, and the victim's guardian has in the master's estate the value of the criminal, meaning if his value is less than the compensation, and it is from all of his property, and the master does not become one who has chosen the ransom. And if each of the two slaves kills a man by mistake, and the case is as before, each of the two slaves works for half of his value, and each of the two victims' guardians has in the master's estate the value of the slave who committed the tort against him, and the master does not become one who has chosen the ransom. All this that we have mentioned is when the master applies the ambiguous manumission to one of his two slaves before the tort. As for when the application of the ambiguous manumission is after the tort: he said: A man has two slaves each worth one thousand, and one of them kills a man by mistake, then the master says in his health 'one of you is free' while knowing of the tort, then the master dies before clarification: half of each of them becomes free, and each of them works for half of his value, and the master becomes one who has chosen the ransom regarding the criminal. Then, when he becomes one who has chosen the ransom, the amount of the value is (paid from) the property. And if each of the two slaves commits a tort, and the case is as before, they work in the manner we described, and he becomes one who has chosen the ransom for both torts, but only one blood-money is obligatory in the master's estate and the value of the two slaves, and that is from all of the property. And what exceeds the value up to the full blood-money is considered from one-third of the property. And the two torts are halves because one is not prior to the other. He said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: A man has two slaves, Salim and Rabi'. Salim kills a man by mistake during the master's health, and the master says 'one of you is free', then Rabi' kills another man during the master's health, then the master dies before clarification: half of each of them becomes free, each of them works for half of his value, and the master is obliged to ransom for the killing of Salim. This is from him a choice for ransom, except that the ransom for Salim in the blood-money is considered from all of the property, and what exceeds that up to the full blood-money is considered from one-third, and he is not obliged to ransom for the killing of Rabi'. And if the master did not say what was mentioned, but the master applies the manumission to Salim, he becomes one who has chosen the ransom for the killing of Salim; and if he applies the manumission to Rabi', he does not become one who has chosen.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a detailed expansion on the rulings concerning ambiguous manumission (al-'itq al-mubham) when a slave commits a tort. It begins by noting that qiyas (analogy) would reject the validity of ambiguous manumission because it does not achieve the full benefit of manumission, but the Hanafis validate it out of necessity for the validity of transactions. The effect of such manumission is limited to what is necessary, which is the life of the slave (his person) but not his limbs or blood-money. The text then presents numerous cases from al-Jami' al-Saghir, each with subtle distinctions. The key principle is that when a master says 'one of you is free' to his slaves, and then a tort occurs, the master's choices and liabilities depend on timing (before or after the tort), whether the master specifies the freed slave, and whether the master dies before clarification. The passage also distinguishes this from analogous cases in sale with option and divorce to show that the master does not automatically become a 'chooser of ransom' (mukhtar li al-fida') in all situations. The core idea is that the master's ambiguous manumission is treated as a transfer from the unknown to the known, limited by necessity, and the liabilities are distributed accordingly.

Key Terms
مختار للفداء (mukhtar li al-fida'): A master who has effectively chosen to pay ransom, thereby becoming liable for the full value of the slave to the victim.
خيار (khiyar): Option in a contract, here applied to the master's choice to hand over or ransom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the master's status if, after ambiguous manumission, he specifies the criminal slave as free?
A) He retains the choice to hand over the slave.
B) He becomes obligated to ransom the slave (mukhtar li al-fida).
C) He is released from all liability.
D) He must pay full diya from his own pocket.

Key Takeaway
When a master specifies the criminal after ambiguous manumission, he automatically becomes liable for ransom; specifying the non-criminal preserves his choice.

Diyat — Blood Money

Master Dies Before Specification: Half-Free Ruling
Explains the consequences when the master dies before specifying the freed slave after ambiguous manumission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the share of freedom and value when a master dies after ambiguous manumission without specifying the freed slave.

Core Idea
If the master dies before specifying, each slave becomes half-free and must earn half their value; the master does not become mukhtar li al-fida.

When a master makes an ambiguous manumission and then dies without clarifying, the slaves become half-free. Each must work (saca) to pay half their value to the master's estate. The victim of a tort gets the value of the criminal slave from the estate, but the master is not considered to have chosen ransom because the manumission was not yet finalized. This lesson walks through the calculation and rationale.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario: death before clarification
The master said 'one of you is free' while healthy, then a tort occurred, then he died without saying which one is free.
2
Determine the share of freedom
Each slave becomes half-free, half-slave. This is because the ambiguous manumission affects each slave equally until specification.
3
Calculate the value owed by each slave
Each slave owes half of his value to the master's estate. They must work (saca) to pay this.
Saca means they work and their earnings go to the estate.
4
Handle the tort victim's claim
The victim's guardian (wali al-jinaya) takes the value of the criminal slave from the master's estate (if less than diya).
The value is taken from the entire estate, not just a third.
5
Confirm that the master does NOT become mukhtar
Because the master died without specifying, he never effectively chose ransom. So the tort liability is paid from the estate, not as ransom.
This differentiates from the case where the master specifies the criminal while alive.

Source Text

العلم
القياس يأبى ثبوت العتق في المجهول لأنه لا يفيد فائدته وإنما صححناه ضرورة صحة التصرف وأثبتنا له ولاية لنقل من المجهول إلى المعلوم فيقدر بقدر الضرورة وهي النفس دون الأطراف والدية فبقي مملوكاً في حقهما فتجب القيمة فيهما فيكون نصفين بين المولى والورثة فيأخذ هو نصف كل واحد منهما ويترك النصف لورثته لأن موجب العتق ثابت في أحدهما في حق المولى فلا بدل له فوزع ذلك عليهما نصفين وإن قتلاهما على التعاقب فعلى قاتل الأول قيمته للمولى لتعينه للرق، وعلى قاتل الثاني ديته لورثته لتعينه للعتق بعد موت الأول. وإن كان لا يدري أيهما قتل أولاً فعلى كل واحد منهما قيمته وللمولى من كل واحد منهما نصف القيمة كالأول لعدم أولوية أحدهما بالتقدم. وفي الجامع الصغير : وإذا قال الرجل لعبدين له في صحته أحدكما حر ثم إن أحدهما قتل رجلاً خطأ فالقاضي يجبر المولى على البيان، فإن أوقع العتق على غير الجاني خير في الثاني بين الدفع والفداء، وإن أوقع العتق على الجاني صار مختاراً للفداء في الجاني فرق بين هذا وبين ما إذا باع عبداً على أنه بالخيار ثلاثة أيام فجنى العبد في يد البائع جناية موجبة للمولى في مدة الخيار بأن قتل رجلاً خطأ فأجاز البائع البيع فيه مع العلم بالجناية لم يصر مختاراً للفداء، وإن أعجز نفسه عن الدفع مع الجناية. وكذا إذا كان الخيار للمشتري فجنى العبد في مدة الخيار ثم رد المشتري العبد لا يكون مختاراً للفداء وإن عجز نفسه عن الدفع بسبب الرد بالجناية. ولو كان كل واحد من العبدين قتل رجلاً خطأ بعد العتق المبهم ثم أوقع المولى العتق على أحدهما بعينه يخير بين الدفع والفداء في العبد الأخير وعليه قيمة العبد الذي أوقع فيه العتق لولي الجناية يريد إذا كانت قيمته أقل من الدية ولم يصر مختاراً للفداء بصرف العتق إلى الجاني، فرق بين هذا وبين ما لو طلق إحدى امرأتيه في صحته ثلاثاً ثم مرض مرض الموت فأجبر على البيان فأوقع ذلك على أحدهما فإنه يصير فاراً وإن كان مضطراً إلى البيان، وكذلك لو كانت جناية أحد العبدين قطع يد وجناية الآخر قتل نفس خطأ كان الجواب كما قلنا ولو قال في صحته لعبدين قيمة كل واحد منهما ألف أحدكما حر ثم قتل أحدهما رجلاً خطأ ثم مات المولى قبل البيان عتق من كل واحد منهما نصفه وسعى كل واحد منهما في نصف قيمته وللمجني عليه في مال المولى قيمة الجاني يريد به إذا كانت قيمته أقل من الأرش ويصير من جميع ماله ولا يصير المولى مختاراً للفداء. ولو كان كل واحد من العبدين قتل رجلاً خطأ والمسألة بحالها سعى كل واحد من العبدين في نصف قيمته ولكل واحد من المجني عليهما في مال المولى قيمة العبد الذي جنى عليه ولم يصر المولى مختاراً للفداء هذا الذي ذكرناه كله إذا أوقع المولى العتق المبهم على أحد عبديه قبل الجناية، وأما إذا كان إيقاع العتق المبهم بعد الجناية فقال : رجل له عبدان قيمة كل واحد منهما ألف فقتل أحدهما قتيلاً خطأ ثم قال المولى في صحته أحدكما حر وهو عالم بالجناية ثم مات المولى قبل البيان عتق من كل واحد منهما نصفه وسعى كل واحد منهما في نصف قيمته ويصير المولى مختاراً للفداء في الجاني، ثم إذا صار مختاراً للفداء فمقدار القيمة المال، وإذا جنى كل واحد من العبدين جناية والمسألة بحالها سعياً على الوجه الذي وصفناه وصار مختاراً للفداء في الجنايتين ولكن تجب دية واحد في مال المولى وقيمة العبدين ويكون ذلك من جميع المال وما زاد على القيمة إلى تمام الدية يعتبر من ثلث المال وتكون الجنايتان نصفين إذ ليس أحدهما أولى من الآخر. قال في الجامع الصغير : رجل له عبدان سالم ورابع فقتل سالم رجلاً خطأ في صحة المولى فقال المولى أحدكما حر ثم قتل رابع رجلاً آخر في صحة المولى ثم مات المولى قبل البيان عتق من كل واحد منهما نصفه وسعى كل واحد منهما في نصف قيمته ولزم المولى الفداء في قتل سالم وهذا منه اختيار للفداء إلا أن فداء سالم في الدية يعتبر من جميع المال وما زاد على ذلك إلى تمام الدية يعتبر من الثلث ولا يلزمه الفداء في قتل رابع ولو أن المولى لم يقل ما ذكر ولكن المولى أوقع العتق على سالم صار مختاراً للفداء في قتل سالم، وإن أوقع المولى العتق على رابع لم يصر مختاراً.
Book of Blood-Money, Chapter on Tort of the Slave and Tort Against Him. Knowledge: Qiyas rejects the establishment of manumission in the ambiguous because it does not achieve its benefit, but we have validated it due to the necessity of validating the transaction. And we established for it authority to transfer from the ambiguous to the known, so it is limited to the extent of necessity, which is the person (life), not the limbs or the blood-money. So he remains a slave regarding them, so value is obligatory for them, so it is in two halves between the master and the heirs. He (the master) takes half of each of them and leaves the half to his heirs, because the effect of manumission is established in one of them regarding the master, so he has no substitute, so that is distributed over them in halves. And if they both kill him sequentially, then upon the killer of the first is his value for the master, because he was determined for slavery, and upon the killer of the second is his blood-money for his heirs, because he was determined for manumission after the death of the first. And if it is not known which one killed first, then upon each of them is his value, and for the master from each of them half the value, as in the first case, because neither has priority over the other in precedence. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: If a man says to two slaves of his in his health 'one of you is free', then one of them kills a man by mistake, the judge compels the master to clarify. Then if he applies the manumission to the non-criminal, he gives the other a choice between handing over and ransom. And if he applies the manumission to the criminal, he becomes one who has chosen the ransom for the criminal. There is a difference between this and the case where he sold a slave with the option of three days, and the slave commits a tort in the hand of the seller that obligates the master during the option period, such as killing a man by mistake, and the seller completes the sale with knowledge of the tort: he does not become one who has chosen the ransom, even though he has disabled himself from handing over due to the tort. Similarly, if the option is for the buyer and the slave commits a tort during the option period, then the buyer returns the slave, he does not become one who has chosen the ransom, even though he has disabled himself from handing over due to the return because of the tort. And if each of the two slaves killed a man by mistake after the ambiguous manumission, then the master applies the manumission to one of them specifically, he is given a choice between handing over and ransom regarding the latter slave, and he owes the value of the slave on whom he applied the manumission to the guardian of the tort, meaning if his value is less than the blood-money, and he does not become one who has chosen the ransom by directing the manumission to the criminal. There is a difference between this and the case where he divorces one of his two wives three times in his health, then falls ill with a mortal illness and is compelled to clarify, and he applies that to one of them: then he becomes one who is fleeing (to avoid inheritance), even though he was forced to clarify. And likewise, if the tort of one of the two slaves is cutting off a hand and the tort of the other is killing a person by mistake, the answer is as we said. And if he says in his health to two slaves each worth one thousand 'one of you is free', then one of them kills a man by mistake, then the master dies before clarification: half of each of them becomes free, and each of them works for half of his value, and the victim's guardian has in the master's estate the value of the criminal, meaning if his value is less than the compensation, and it is from all of his property, and the master does not become one who has chosen the ransom. And if each of the two slaves kills a man by mistake, and the case is as before, each of the two slaves works for half of his value, and each of the two victims' guardians has in the master's estate the value of the slave who committed the tort against him, and the master does not become one who has chosen the ransom. All this that we have mentioned is when the master applies the ambiguous manumission to one of his two slaves before the tort. As for when the application of the ambiguous manumission is after the tort: he said: A man has two slaves each worth one thousand, and one of them kills a man by mistake, then the master says in his health 'one of you is free' while knowing of the tort, then the master dies before clarification: half of each of them becomes free, and each of them works for half of his value, and the master becomes one who has chosen the ransom regarding the criminal. Then, when he becomes one who has chosen the ransom, the amount of the value is (paid from) the property. And if each of the two slaves commits a tort, and the case is as before, they work in the manner we described, and he becomes one who has chosen the ransom for both torts, but only one blood-money is obligatory in the master's estate and the value of the two slaves, and that is from all of the property. And what exceeds the value up to the full blood-money is considered from one-third of the property. And the two torts are halves because one is not prior to the other. He said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: A man has two slaves, Salim and Rabi'. Salim kills a man by mistake during the master's health, and the master says 'one of you is free', then Rabi' kills another man during the master's health, then the master dies before clarification: half of each of them becomes free, each of them works for half of his value, and the master is obliged to ransom for the killing of Salim. This is from him a choice for ransom, except that the ransom for Salim in the blood-money is considered from all of the property, and what exceeds that up to the full blood-money is considered from one-third, and he is not obliged to ransom for the killing of Rabi'. And if the master did not say what was mentioned, but the master applies the manumission to Salim, he becomes one who has chosen the ransom for the killing of Salim; and if he applies the manumission to Rabi', he does not become one who has chosen.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a detailed expansion on the rulings concerning ambiguous manumission (al-'itq al-mubham) when a slave commits a tort. It begins by noting that qiyas (analogy) would reject the validity of ambiguous manumission because it does not achieve the full benefit of manumission, but the Hanafis validate it out of necessity for the validity of transactions. The effect of such manumission is limited to what is necessary, which is the life of the slave (his person) but not his limbs or blood-money. The text then presents numerous cases from al-Jami' al-Saghir, each with subtle distinctions. The key principle is that when a master says 'one of you is free' to his slaves, and then a tort occurs, the master's choices and liabilities depend on timing (before or after the tort), whether the master specifies the freed slave, and whether the master dies before clarification. The passage also distinguishes this from analogous cases in sale with option and divorce to show that the master does not automatically become a 'chooser of ransom' (mukhtar li al-fida') in all situations. The core idea is that the master's ambiguous manumission is treated as a transfer from the unknown to the known, limited by necessity, and the liabilities are distributed accordingly.

Key Terms
سعى (sa'a): The slave works to earn money to pay his ransom or part of his value. Here, each half-free slave works to pay half his value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the master dies before specification, how much of each slave becomes free?
A) The entire slave becomes free.
B) Half of each slave becomes free.
C) One slave becomes fully free, the other remains slave.
D) No freedom occurs.

Key Takeaway
Death before specification results in half-freedom for each slave, and the master does not become mukhtar li al-fida.

Tort After Ambiguous Manumission: Mukhtar li al-Fida'
Explains the ruling when ambiguous manumission occurs after the tort.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a master becomes mukhtar li al-fida' when a tort occurs after ambiguous manumission, and calculate the liability from the estate.

Core Idea
If the tort occurs before the ambiguous manumission, the master may or may not become mukhtar. If the tort occurs after, the master becomes mukhtar li al-fida' automatically.

Timing matters. If the master says 'one of you is free' after a tort has already occurred (with knowledge), then the master becomes mukhtar li al-fida' (chooser of ransom) for the criminal slave, even if he dies before specification. This lesson explores that scenario and the calculations for multiple torts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the timing: tort before manumission
The master says 'one of you is free' after a tort has already occurred, and he knows about it.
This is the opposite of the earlier cases where tort came after manumission.
2
Determine the effect on the master's status
The master becomes mukhtar li al-fida' for the criminal because he chose to make a statement that prevents handing over the slave.
He could have specified the non-criminal, but his ambiguous statement makes him liable.
3
Calculate the liability when one tort occurs
When the master dies, each slave is half-free and pays half his value. The victim receives the slave's value from the estate, and the master is mukhtar, so the value is paid from all property (not just one-third).
If value < diya, the victim gets only the value.
4
Extend to two torts by both slaves
If both slaves commit torts after the ambiguous statement, the master becomes mukhtar for both. Only one diya is owed from the estate, plus the values of both slaves are taken from all property. Any excess over values up to full diya comes from one-third.
The two torts are treated as halves.
5
Distinguish from the earlier case of tort before manumission
In the earlier case (lesson 2), the master did not become mukhtar automatically because he could still hand over the slave. Here, he is aware of the tort and his statement forecloses that option.
