Master's Voluntary Payment and Subsequent Crime: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on effect of voluntary payment of value to first victim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn regarding the liability of the first victim when the master voluntarily pays the mudabbar's value, and apply the ruling to determine the second victim's options.

Core Idea
A voluntary payment by the master does not extinguish the later victim's claim; the second can choose to pursue the master or the first recipient.

When a master owns a mudabbar slave who commits crimes, the master is liable to pay a single value shared among all victims. But what happens if the master voluntarily pays the entire value to the first victim before a second crime occurs? Does the second victim have recourse only against the master, or also against the first victim? This passage presents a sharp intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, with significant practical consequences for how justice is served.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text distinguishes between payment by court order (qada') and voluntary payment without court order (bi-ghayr qada'). In the first scenario, the master is compelled, and the second victim must pursue the first. In the second scenario, a dispute arises.
This distinction triggers the ikhtilaf.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
When payment is voluntary, Abu Hanifa gives the second victim an option: either pursue the master for his share of the value, or pursue the first victim who received the payment.
The first victim is considered to have received the second's share wrongfully.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the first victim is not liable because he received the payment rightfully at the time; the master did what a judge would have ordered, and there was no second crime when payment was made.
They consider the master's payment valid and final regarding the first victim.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Abu Hanifa's argument is that all crimes of the mudabbar create a single value obligation, and all victims are partners. By paying voluntarily, the master acted without authority over the second's share, so the payment is ineffective as to that share. This creates a right for the second to recover from either party, ensuring only one value is ultimately paid.
The key concept is the legal fiction that later crimes are considered contemporaneous in the division of value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في باب غصب العبد والمدبر والصبي والجناية في ذلك الألف الأولى في المرتهن قال رحمه الله : (فإن دفع القيمة بقضاء فجنى أخرى شارك الثاني الأول) يعني إذا دفع المولى القيمة لولي الجناية الأولى بقضاء القاضي ثم جناية أخرى بعد ذلك فلا شيء على المولى لأن جناياته كلها لا توجب إلا قيمة واحدة ولا تعدي من المولى بدفعها إلى ولي الجناية الأولى لأنه مجبور عليه بالقضاء فيتبع ولي الجناية الثانية ولي الأول فيشاركه فيها ويقتسماها على قدر حقهما على ما ذكرنا قال رحمه الله : (ولو بغير قضاء اتبع السيد أو ولي الجناية) أي لو دفع المولى القيمة إلى ولي الجناية الأولى بغير قضاء كان ولي الجناية الثانية بالخيار إن شاء اتبع المولى بحصته من القيمة وإن شاء اتبع ولي الجناية الأولى وهذا عند أبي حنيفة . وقالا : لا شيء على الولي لأنه فعل عين ما يفعله القاضي ولا تعدي منه بتسليمه إلى الأول لأنه حين دفع الحق إلى مستحقه لم تكن الجناية الثانية موجودة ولا علم له بما يحدث حتى يجعل متعدياً. ولأبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى أن جنايات المدبر توجب قيمة واحدة وهم شركاء فيها والجناية المتأخرة كالمقارنة حكماً ولهذا يشتركون فيها كلهم جميعاً، ثم إذا دفعها إلى الأول باختياره صار متعدياً في حق الثاني لأن حصته وجبت عليه وليس له ولاية عليه، فإذا لم ينفذ دفع المولى في حق الثاني فالثاني بالخيار إن شاء تبع الأول لأنه قبض حقه ظلماً فصار به ضامناً فيأخذه منه، وإن شاء اتبع المولى لأنه دفع حقه بغير إذنه فإذا أخذ منه رجع المولى على الأول بما ضمن للثاني وهو حصته لأنه قبضه بغير حق فيسترده منه، وهذا لأنه لا يجب عليه إلا قيمة واحدة فلو لم يكن له حق الرجوع لكان الواجب عليه أكثر من القيمة، ولأن الثاني مقارنة من وجه حتى يشاركه ومتأخرة من وجه في حق اعتبار القيمة فيعتبر مقارنة في حق التضمين أيضاً كيلا يبطل حق ولي الثانية.
With regard to the chapter on usurpation of the slave, mudabbar, and minor, and crimes regarding that: the first thousand is for the mortgagee. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: "If he pays the value by court order, then another crime occurs, the second shares with the first." Meaning: if the master pays the value to the guardian of the first crime by the judge's decree, then another crime occurs afterward, there is nothing upon the master because all his crimes only require one value, and the master is not at fault by paying it to the first guardian because he was compelled by the court. So the guardian of the second crime pursues the first guardian and shares with him in the value, and they divide it according to their rights as we mentioned. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: "And if without court order, the master or the crime's guardian may be pursued." That is, if the master pays the value to the first guardian without a court order, the second guardian has the option: if he wishes, he pursues the master for his share of the value; if he wishes, he pursues the first guardian. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: There is nothing upon the guardian because he did exactly what the judge would do, and he is not at fault by delivering it to the first since at the time he paid the right to its entitled recipient, the second crime did not exist and he had no knowledge of what would happen, so he is not considered a wrongdoer. Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, argued: The crimes of the mudabbar require one value, and they are partners in it; the later crime is like an earlier one in legal effect, therefore they all share in it. Then when he paid it to the first voluntarily, he became a wrongdoer regarding the right of the second because his share became obligatory on him and he has no authority over him. Since the master's payment is not effective regarding the second, the second has the option: if he wishes, he follows the first because he received his right unjustly, so he becomes liable thereby and takes it from him; if he wishes, he follows the master because he paid his right without his permission. If he takes from the master, the master then recovers from the first what he guaranteed to the second, i.e., his share, because he received it without right and so he takes it back. This is because only one value is obligatory on him; if he did not have the right of recourse, more than the value would be obligatory. And because the second is in one respect like an earlier creditor so that he shares, and in another respect later regarding the consideration of value, so he is considered earlier in respect of liability also, so that the right of the second guardian is not nullified.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario when a master pays the value of the mudabbar to the first crime's victim either by court order or voluntarily, and then a second crime occurs. If payment was by court order, the master is free of further liability; the second victim must seek his share from the first victim. If payment was voluntary without court order, there is a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Abu Hanifa holds that the second victim has a choice: pursue the master (for his share) or pursue the first victim (who received the money unjustly regarding the second's share). The Sahibayn hold that the first victim bears no liability because he received the payment rightfully at the time, and the master was not at fault. The reasoning of Abu Hanifa is that all crimes of the mudabbar require only one value, and all victims are partners. When the master paid voluntarily, he acted without authority regarding the second's share, so the payment is ineffective as to that share. Therefore, the second can recover from the master, who then can take back from the first. This ensures that only one value is ultimately paid, and the second's right is not lost.

Key Terms
اتباع (ittiba'): To pursue or seek recourse against someone for a debt or share.
تعدي (ta'di): Wrongful act or transgression against another's right.
ولي الجناية (wali al-jinayah): The guardian or representative of the crime victim, entitled to receive blood money or compensation.
جناية (jinayah): A crime or tort that incurs monetary liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a master voluntarily pays the mudabbar's value to the first victim without court order, and then a second crime occurs, the second victim can:
A) Only pursue the master
B) Only pursue the first victim
C) Pursue either the master or the first victim
D) Cannot claim anything because the master already paid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi jurisprudence, when the master voluntarily pays the mudabbar's value to the first victim, a subsequent crime gives the second victim a choice to pursue either the master or the first victim, preventing the master from avoiding full liability.

Emancipation of Mudabbar After Crime: One Value Only
Ruling on emancipation after crime

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that only one value is due from the master when a mudabbar is emancipated after committing a crime, and explain the legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Emancipation after crime does not multiply liability; only one value is due.

This lesson examines the intersection of emancipation and pre-existing crimes. When a master emancipates a mudabbar who has already committed a crime, the master's liability is limited. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between rights attached to the slave and those on the master.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actors
Recognize that the mudabbar is a slave promised emancipation upon the master's death. The crime is committed before emancipation.
2
State the ruling
After emancipation, the master is liable for only one value (the slave's original price), not combined with diyat.
This is regardless of the number or severity of crimes.
3
Trace the reasoning (ta'leel)
The master's right was not attached to the slave, so emancipation does not defeat the victims' rights. One value suffices because the slave was not rendered deficient by emancipation.
The reasoning prevents double recovery.
4
Extend to umm walad
The same rule applies to umm walad (slave mother of master's child) – she is like the mudabbar in this regard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أعتق المدبر وقد جنى جناية لم يلزمه إلا قيمة واحدة لما ذكرنا، وسواء أعتقه بعد العلم بالجناية أو قبله لأن حق المولى لم يتعلق بالعبد فلم يكن مفوتاً بالإعتاق وأم الولد كالمدبر، وإذا أقر المدبر وأم الولد بجناية توجب المال لم يجز إقراره وجنايته على المولى لا على نفسه وإقراره على المولى غير نافذ بخلاف ما إذا كانت الجناية موجبة للقود بأن أقر بالقتل عمداً حيث يصح إقراره فيقتل به لأن إقراره على نفسه فينفذ عليه لعدم التهمة .
And if the mudabbar is emancipated after having committed a crime, nothing is due from him except one value, as we mentioned. It is the same whether the master emancipates him after knowledge of the crime or before, because the master's right was not attached to the slave, so he was not making him deficient by emancipation. The umm walad is like the mudabbar. And if the mudabbar or umm walad confesses to a crime that necessitates financial payment, his confession is not valid. His crime is against the master, not against himself, and his confession against the master is not effective. However, if the crime necessitates qisas, such as confessing to intentional killing, his confession is valid, and he is killed for it, because his confession is against himself, so it is effective against him, due to the absence of suspicion.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage covers two rules: (1) If a mudabbar commits a crime and then is emancipated, only one value payment is due from the master, regardless of whether the master knew of the crime at the time of emancipation. This is because the master's right over the slave was not attached to the slave personally; the emancipation does not defeat the victims' rights. (2) The mudabbar's confession to a crime requiring financial compensation (such as accidental killing or injury) is invalid because the liability falls on the master, not the slave. The slave's confession cannot bind the master. However, if the crime requires qisas (retaliation), such as intentional killing, the confession is valid and the slave can be executed. This is because qisas is a personal right against the slave himself, and his confession is against his own self, which is permissible.

Key Terms
مُدَبَّر (mudabbar): A slave who is promised emancipation upon the master's death.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the liability of a master who emancipates a mudabbar after the mudabbar has committed a crime requiring financial payment?
A) The master must pay full diyat and the slave's value
B) The master must pay only one value (the slave's price)
C) The master must pay nothing because the slave's crime is forgiven
D) The master must pay half the diyat

Key Takeaway
Emancipation after a crime does not multiply the master's liability; only one value is due, regardless of knowledge or number of crimes.

Confession of Mudabbar/Umm Walad: Financial vs. Qisas Crimes
Rulings on confession by mudabbar/umm walad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the validity of a mudabbar's confession to a financial crime versus a qisas crime, and explain the underlying rationale.

Core Idea
A mudabbar's confession to financial crime is void because it binds the master; confessions to qisas are valid as they concern his own life.

This lesson examines the second part of the passage: the rules of confession by a mudabbar or umm walad. The key distinction is whether the crime requires financial compensation or corporal punishment. The Hanafi ruling protects the master from the slave's admissions while respecting the slave's personal accountability for crimes against life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of crime confessed to
Distinguish between crimes that necessitate financial payment (e.g., accidental killing) and those that require qisas (e.g., intentional killing).
Financial crimes include diyat and damages; qisas crimes involve retribution.
2
State the ruling for financial crime confession
The mudabbar's confession to a financial crime is invalid and not binding on the master. The crime is against the master, not the slave.
The slave cannot affect the master's rights by his own admission.
3
State the ruling for qisas confession
The confession is valid and the slave is executed. The reason is that qisas is a personal right against the slave himself.
No suspicion exists that the slave is harming another's interest.
4
Understand the ta'leel (rationale)
For financial crimes: liability falls on the master, so the slave's confession would harm the master. For qisas: the confession harms only the slave himself, which is permissible.
Key concept: التهمة (suspicion) is absent in self-harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if the mudabbar is emancipated after having committed a crime, nothing is due from him except one value, as we mentioned. This passage covers two rules: (1) If a mudabbar commits a crime and then is emancipated, only one value payment is due from the master, regardless of whether the master knew of the crime at the time of emancipation. This is because the master's right over the slave was not attached to the slave personally; the emancipation does not defeat the victims' rights. (2) The mudabbar's confession to a crime requiring financial compensation (such as accidental killing or injury) is invalid because the liability falls on the master, not the slave. Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Confession by a person against himself regarding a right of another.
قود (qisas): Retaliation, i.e., execution for intentional murder or similar.

Understanding the Structure of the Chapter on Ghasb and Crimes of Slaves and Mudabbar
Commentary on chapter arrangement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical sequence of topics in the chapter on ghasb and crimes of slaves, mudabbar, and minors as articulated by Hanafi commentators.

Core Idea
The chapter's structure follows a logical progression from simple crime to composite crime with usurpation, then to usurpation alone.

This passage is a brief introduction by the commentator, explaining why the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq arranged the chapter on usurpation (ghasb) of slaves, mudabbar, and minors together with their crimes. By understanding the commentator's reasoning, we gain insight into how Hanafi jurists organize legal material for clarity and pedagogical effectiveness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quoted sources
The commentator quotes two works: al-Nihaya and Ghayat al-Bayan. Note that these are commentaries on earlier texts, providing structural explanations.
Al-Nihaya is a commentary by al-Sarakhsi on al-Shaybani's works; Ghayat al-Bayan is by al-Tahawi.
2
Extract the principle from al-Nihaya
Al-Nihaya states that after discussing the mudabbar's crime liability in the previous section, this chapter covers what is due from him and to him when he is wrongfully taken.
This indicates a move from discussing the mudabbar as a perpetrator to discussing him as a victim of ghasb.
3
Extract the principle from Ghayat al-Bayan
Ghayat al-Bayan explains the order: the author first discusses the crime of the slave and mudabbar (singular), then the composite case where crime and usurpation are combined, and finally the ruling on usurpation alone.
The singular comes before the composite, and then the author extends to the pure usurpation ruling.
4
Summarize the logical flow
The chapter progresses from simple (crime of the slave) to compound (crime with usurpation) to general (usurpation of any such property). This is a common pedagogical method in Hanafi fiqh to build from specific to general.
5
Explain the pedagogical rationale
By starting with the crime of the slave (a known ruling) and then adding the element of usurpation, the author teaches how multiple legal categories interact. Finally, he gives the standalone ruling on usurpation, which applies to all cases.
This structure helps the student see how fiqh rulings relate and build on each other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب غصب العبد والمدبر والصبي والجناية في ذلك قال في النهاية : لما ذكر حكم المدبر في الجناية ذكر في هذا الباب ما يرد عليه وما يرد منه وذكر حكم ما يلحق به ا هـ . وقال في غاية البيان لما ذكر جناية العبد والمدبر ذكر في هذا الباب جنايتهما مع غصبهما لأن المفرد قبل المركب ثم جر كلامه إلى بيان حكم غصب
Chapter on the usurpation of the slave, the mudabbar, and the minor, and crimes regarding that. He said in al-Nihaya: When he mentioned the ruling of the mudabbar regarding crimes, he mentioned in this chapter what comes from him and what happens to him, and he mentioned the ruling of what is attached to it, end. And he said in Ghayat al-Bayan: When he mentioned the crime of the slave and the mudabbar, he mentioned in this chapter their crimes together with their usurpation because the singular comes before the composite, then he extended his speech to explain the ruling of usurpation.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This is a brief introduction to the next chapter of the book, which deals with cases where a slave, mudabbar, or minor is usurped (wrongfully taken) and also commits crimes. The commentator quotes two sources: al-Nihaya explains that after discussing the mudabbar's crime liability in the previous section, this chapter covers what is due from him and to him (i.e., when someone else wrongs the slave). Ghayat al-Bayan explains the order: the author discusses the crimes of the slave and mudabbar first (singular), then moves to crimes combined with usurpation (composite), and then to the ruling of usurpation itself. This gives the student an understanding of the book's structure.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Wrongful taking or usurpation of another's property without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because the author forgot to discuss crime later
D) Because mudabbar crimes are the main focus

Key Takeaway
Hanafi jurists organize chapters from simple to composite, as exemplified by the progression from crime to crime-with-usurpation to usurpation alone.

Multiple Tort Claims Against a Mudabbar
Explains the ruling on paying compensation for a mudabbar's multiple torts with and without court order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that multiple torts by a mudabbar only require a single value and distinguish between payment by court order and voluntary payment when later torts arise.

Core Idea
Multiple torts by a mudabbar only require one value; voluntary payment makes the master liable to later victims.

The mudabbar is a unique category in Hanafi fiqh: a slave whose freedom is delayed till the master's death. This lesson examines what happens when such a slave commits multiple torts. The key principle: all torts together only demand the slave's value once. But how is that value distributed among victims, especially if the master pays voluntarily? The Hanafi school has a detailed discussion, with a notable difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on multiple torts
Determine the maximum liability for a mudabbar's torts. The master owes only one value regardless of the number of torts.
This is the foundational principle from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Distinguish court-ordered vs. voluntary payment
When the master pays by court order, he is compelled and not at fault. When he pays voluntarily, he acts on his own and may be considered a transgressor.
This difference triggers the intra-madhhab dispute.
3
Apply the rule: second victim's remedies
With court order: second victim shares with first in the paid value. With voluntary payment: second victim has the option to pursue either the master or the first victim (Abu Hanifa's view).
According to Sahibayn, the first victim is not liable.
4
Analyse the intra-Hanafi dispute
Why do Abu Yusuf and Muhammad dissent? They argue the first victim received payment for an existing tort and the master's payment was analogous to a court order.
Abu Hanifa's response: voluntary payment is a transgression against the second victim's right because later torts are concurrent in legal effect.
5
Extend the ruling to emancipation and umm walad
Emancipation before or after the tort does not increase liability; the master still owes only one value. The same rule applies to an umm walad.
This reinforces the single-value principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات باب غصب العبد والمدبر والصبي والجناية في ذلك
في باب غصب العبد والمدبر والصبي والجناية في ذلك
الألف الأولى في المرتهن قال رحمه الله : (فإن دفع القيمة بقضاء فجنى أخرى شارك الثاني الأول) يعني إذا دفع المولى القيمة لولي الجناية الأولى بقضاء القاضي ثم جناية أخرى بعد ذلك فلا شيء على المولى لأن جناياته كلها لا توجب إلا قيمة واحدة ولا تعدي من المولى بدفعها إلى ولي الجناية الأولى لأنه مجبور عليه بالقضاء فيتبع ولي الجناية الثانية ولي الأول فيشاركه فيها ويقتسماها على قدر حقهما على ما ذكرنا قال رحمه الله : (ولو بغير قضاء اتبع السيد أو ولي الجناية) أي لو دفع المولى القيمة إلى ولي الجناية الأولى بغير قضاء كان ولي الجناية الثانية بالخيار إن شاء اتبع المولى بحصته من القيمة وإن شاء اتبع ولي الجناية الأولى وهذا عند أبي حنيفة . وقالا : لا شيء على الولي لأنه فعل عين ما يفعله القاضي ولا تعدي منه بتسليمه إلى الأول لأنه حين دفع الحق إلى مستحقه لم تكن الجناية الثانية موجودة ولا علم له بما يحدث حتى يجعل متعدياً. ولأبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى أن جنايات المدبر توجب قيمة واحدة وهم شركاء فيها والجناية المتأخرة كالمقارنة حكماً ولهذا يشتركون فيها كلهم جميعاً، ثم إذا دفعها إلى الأول باختياره صار متعدياً في حق الثاني لأن حصته وجبت عليه وليس له ولاية عليه، فإذا لم ينفذ دفع المولى في حق الثاني فالثاني بالخيار إن شاء تبع الأول لأنه قبض حقه ظلماً فصار به ضامناً فيأخذه منه، وإن شاء اتبع المولى لأنه دفع حقه بغير إذنه فإذا أخذ منه رجع المولى على الأول بما ضمن للثاني وهو حصته لأنه قبضه بغير حق فيسترده منه، وهذا لأنه لا يجب عليه إلا قيمة واحدة فلو لم يكن له حق الرجوع لكان الواجب عليه أكثر من القيمة، ولأن الثاني مقارنة من وجه حتى يشاركه ومتأخرة من وجه في حق اعتبار القيمة فيعتبر مقارنة في حق التضمين أيضاً كيلا يبطل حق ولي الثانية. وإذا أعتق المدبر وقد جنى جناية لم يلزمه إلا قيمة واحدة لما ذكرنا، وسواء أعتقه بعد العلم بالجناية أو قبله لأن حق المولى لم يتعلق بالعبد فلم يكن مفوتاً بالإعتاق وأم الولد كالمدبر
The Book of Blood Money, Chapter on Usurpation of the Slave, the Mudabbar, the Minor, and Torts Related to That. The first letter in [the discussion of] the pledgee. He (the author) said: 'If he pays the value by a court order and then [the slave] commits another tort, the second [victim] shares with the first.' Meaning: if the master pays the value to the first victim's guardian by a court order, and then there is another tort after that, nothing is owed by the master because all the torts of [the mudabbar] only necessitate a single value, and the master is not at fault for paying it to the first victim's guardian because he is compelled by the court order. So the second victim's guardian follows the first guardian, sharing in that [value] and dividing it according to their respective rights as mentioned. He (the author) said: 'And if [he pays] without a court order, the master or the first victim's guardian may be pursued.' That is, if the master pays the value to the first victim's guardian without a court order, then the second victim's guardian has the option: if he wishes, he may pursue the master for his share of the value, and if he wishes, he may pursue the first victim's guardian. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Nothing is owed by the [first] guardian because he did exactly what a judge would do, and he is not at fault for delivering it to the first since when he paid the right to its entitled recipient, the second tort did not exist and he had no knowledge of what would happen so as to be considered transgressing. And for Abu Hanifa: The torts of a mudabbar necessitate only one value, and they are partners in it, and the later tort is considered concurrent in legal effect. Hence all of them share in it collectively. Then if [the master] pays it to the first by his choice, he becomes a transgressor with respect to the second because his share became due and the master has no authority over him. So when the master's payment is ineffective regarding the second, the second has the option: if he wishes, he pursues the first because he received his right unjustly and thus becomes liable for it, so he takes it from him; and if he wishes, he pursues the master because he paid his right without his permission. Then if he takes from him, the master has recourse against the first for what he guaranteed to the second, which is his share, because he received it without right and thus takes it back from him. This is because only one value is due from him, so if he had no right of recourse, he would owe more than the value. And because the second is concurrent in one respect to share with [the first] and later in another respect regarding consideration of value, so he is considered concurrent also regarding liability so that the right of the second's guardian is not nullified. And if [the master] emancipates the mudabbar after he has committed a tort, he is only liable for one value as we mentioned, whether he emancipated him after knowledge of the tort or before, because the master's right was not attached to the slave, so he has not caused loss by emancipation. And the umm walad is like the mudabbar.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation and an intra-madhhab dispute. It opens with the ruling on multiple torts committed by a mudabbar (a slave whose emancipation is delayed until the master's death). The master is only liable for one value for all torts, because the mudabbar's entire value is the maximum that can be demanded. Two scenarios are discussed: (1) If the master pays the value to the first victim by court order, then later another tort appears, the second victim shares proportionally with the first in that same paid value. The master is not liable for more. (2) If the master pays voluntarily without court order, then there is a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). According to Abu Hanifa, the second victim has the choice to pursue either the master for his share or the first victim who received the payment. According to the two, the first victim is not liable because he rightfully received payment for a then-existing tort, and the master's payment was valid. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that all torts are concurrent in legal effect, so the master's voluntary payment to the first is a transgression against the second's right. The text then extends the ruling: if the master emancipates the mudabbar after the tort, he still owes only one value; emancipation before or after knowledge makes no difference because the master's right was not attached to the slave. The same rule applies to an umm walad (mother of the master's child). This is a ruling with a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the nature of liability and legal concurrence.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose emancipation is tied to the master's death; until then he remains a slave but cannot be sold.
جناية (jinaayah): A tort or crime, here specifically an act causing harm that requires compensation.
ولي الجناية (wali al-jinaayah): The guardian or legal representative of the victim entitled to compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a master pays the value for a mudabbar's tort voluntarily (without court order) and then another tort appears, what options does the second victim have?
A) He can only pursue the master for his share.
B) He can only pursue the first victim for his share.
C) He may choose to pursue either the master or the first victim.
D) He cannot claim anything because the torts are concurrent.

Key Takeaway
A mudabbar's multiple torts only obligate the master to pay one value, and voluntary payment without court order gives later victims a choice to pursue the master or the earlier recipient.

Does Usurpation Cut Off Transference? The Hanafi Dispute on Liability for a Pre-Existing Wound
Intra-Hanafi dispute on effect of ghasb on sirayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two scenarios of a usurped slave with a cut hand, identify the Hanafi position on whether usurpation cuts off transference, and explain the reasoning behind the relied-upon view.

Core Idea
Usurpation does not automatically cut off transference; liability for a pre-existing wound continues until compensation is owed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a critical nuance in the law of ghasb: when a slave with a pre-existing wound is usurped and then dies from that wound, who is liable? The author presents two scenarios and then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute over whether the act of usurpation itself severs the causal link (sirayah) of the injury. Understanding this ruling sharpens your ability to apply principles of damage liability across changing possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish between (1) master cuts hand before usurpation, then slave dies, and (2) master cuts hand while slave is already in usurper's possession.
The ruling differs dramatically based on timing.
2
State the default ruling for each scenario
In scenario 1, usurper pays value of a cut-hand slave. In scenario 2, master is deemed to have retaken possession and usurper is free.
3
Locate the disputed principle
The key issue: does usurpation itself cut off transference (sirayah), meaning the death is attributed to the usurper's possession, or is the master's original injury still the legal cause?
Al-Hidayah says yes (like sale); Qadikhan and Jami' al-Saghir say no.
4
Analyze the proofs and tarjih
The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) sides with Qadikhan: transference is not cut off until the usurper becomes liable for the value (by court order or consent). The master's hand remains legally due to sirayah, so the usurper's hand does not fully replace it.
The mufta bihi view: usurper is liable but only for the reduced value, and master retains responsibility via sirayah until compensation is owed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (غصب محجور مثله فمات في يده ضمن مدبر جنى عند غاصبه ثم عند الصبي تدبر . اهـ.
قال رحمه الله : (قطع يد عبده فغصبه رجل ومات منه ضمن قيمته اقطع وإن قطع يده في يد الغاصب فمات منه برئ لأن الغصب يوجب ضمان ما غصب ففي المسألة الأولى لما قطعه المولى نقصت قيمته بالقطع فيجب على الغاصب قيمته أقطع ، وفي الثانية حين قطع المولى العبد في يد الغاصب صار مسترداً له لاستيلائه عليه وبرئ الغاصب من ضمانه لوصول ملكه إلى يده. قال صاحب الهداية في الفرق بين المسألتين : إن الغصب قاطع للسراية لأن الملك كالبيع فيصير كأنه هلك بآفة سماوية فتجب قيمته أقطع ولو لم يوجد القاطع في الفصل الثاني فكانت السراية مضافة إلى البداية فصار المولى متلفاً فيصير مسترداً وهذا مشكل، لأن السراية إنما تنقطع باعتبار تبدل الملك لاختلاف المستحقين والغصب ليس بسبب وضعاً والغاصب لا يملك إلا بأداء الضمان ضرورة كيلا يجتمع البدلان في ملك واحد وذلك بعد ملك المولى البدل ولم يوجد تحقيقه أن معنى قولهم بقطع السراية أن ما حصل من بالسراية يكون هدراً إلا أن تسبب ذلك إلى غير الجاني. واعترض عليه الإمام قاضيخان بأن هذا يخالف مذهبنا، فإن الغصب لا يقطع السراية ما لم يملك البدل على الغاصب بقضاء أو رضا لأن السراية أو رضا لأن السراية إنما تنقطع به باعتبار تبدل الملك، وإنما يتبدل الملك به إذا ملك البدل على الغاصب وهو قيمة العبد أقطع أما قبله فلا يضمن. وفي رهن الجامع الصغير في الباب الثاني من جناياته : إنما يضمن الغاصب هنا قيمة العبد لأن السراية وإن لم تنقطع بالغصب وردت على مال متقوم فوجب سبب الضمان فلا يبرأ عنه الغاصب إلا إذا ارتفع الغصب والشيء إنما يرتفع بما هو فوقه أو مثله ويد الغاصب ثابتة على المغصوب حقيقة وحكماً ويد المولى ثابتة عليه حكماً باعتبار السراية لا حقيقة لأن بعد الغصب لم تثبت يده على العبد حقيقة والثابت حكماً دون الثابت حقيقة وحكماً فلم يرتفع الغصب باتصال السراية فقصر عليه الضمان . قال صاحب العناية : فيه نظر لأنا لا نسلم أن يد الغاصب عليه ثابتة حكماً فإن يد المولى ثابتة عليه حكماً ولا يثبت على الشيء الواحد يدان حكميان بكمالهما. أقول نظره ساقط إذ لا وجه لمنع ثبوت يد الغاصب عليه حكماً فإن معنى ثبوت اليد على الشيء حكماً أن يترتب على تلك اليد حكماً من الأحكام وقد ترتب على يد الغاصب فيما نحن فيه وحوب الضمان ،بالإجماع، وأما يد منعه فليس بتام أيضاً إذ لا محذور في أن يثبت على الشيء الواحد يدان حكميان بكمالهما من جهتين مختلفتين وهنا كذلك فإن ثبوت يد المولى على العبد المغصوب منه حكماً باعتبار سراية القطع الذي صدر منه وثبوت يد الغاصب عليه حكماً باعتبار ثبوت يده عليه حقيقة فاختلفت الجهتان
He (the author) said: 'Usurpation of a minor like him, and he dies in his hand, he guarantees [the value]. A mudabbar commits a tort while with the usurper, then with the minor, then emancipation.' He (the author) said: 'He cut off his slave's hand, then a man usurped him, and he died from it: he guarantees his value as one with a cut hand. And if he cuts his hand while in the hand of the usurper, and he dies from it, he is free [from liability] because usurpation necessitates guarantee of what was usurped. In the first case, when the master cut him, his value decreased by the cut, so the usurper must pay his value as one with a cut hand. In the second case, when the master cut the slave in the hand of the usurper, he became retaking possession of him due to his control over him, and the usurper is released from guarantee because his property reached his hand.' The author of al-Hidayah said in differentiating the two cases: 'Indeed, usurpation cuts off the transference because ownership is like sale, so it becomes as if he perished by a heavenly calamity, so his value as one with a cut hand is due. And if there were no cutter in the second case, then the transference would be attributed to the beginning, so the master becomes a destroyer and thus becomes retaking possession.' This is problematic because transference is only cut off by change of ownership due to different entitled parties, and usurpation is not a cause legally, and the usurper only owns by paying the compensation out of necessity so that two compensations do not combine in one estate, and that is after the master owns the compensation, which did not occur. The reality is that the meaning of their statement 'cutting off the transference' is that what is obtained by transference is wasted unless it is caused by someone other than the criminal. Imam Qadikhan objected to this: 'This contradicts our school, for usurpation does not cut off transference as long as the compensation has not become owed on the usurper by court order or consent, because transference is only cut off by change of ownership, and ownership changes only when the compensation becomes owed on the usurper, which is the value of the slave as one with a cut hand. But before that, he is not liable.' And in Rahn al-Jami' al-Saghir in the second chapter of torts: 'The usurper here guarantees the value of the slave because even though transference is not cut off by usurpation, it falls upon valuable property, so the cause of guarantee is established. The usurper is not released from it unless the usurpation is removed, and a thing is only removed by what is above it or like it. And the usurper's hand is established over the usurped property in reality and legally, and the master's hand is established over it legally by virtue of transference, not in reality. Because after usurpation, his hand is not established over the slave in reality, and what is established legally is less than what is established both in reality and legally. So the usurpation is not removed by the connection of transference, so guarantee is confined to him.' The author of al-Inayah said: 'There is consideration in this, for we do not accept that the usurper's hand is established legally over it, because the master's hand is established legally over it, and two complete legal hands cannot be established over one thing.' I say his consideration is invalid because there is no reason to deny the establishment of the usurper's hand legally. Indeed, the meaning of establishing a hand over a thing legally is that certain rulings are attached to that hand, and here the obligation of compensation is attached to the usurper's hand by consensus. As for the hand that prevents it, it is not complete either, because there is no harm in establishing two complete legal hands over one thing from two different aspects. And here it is so: the master's hand over the usurped slave is legally established by virtue of the transference of the cut that originated from him, and the usurper's hand over it is legally established by virtue of his actual hand being established over it. So the two aspects differ.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling and commentary on the liability of a usurper (ghasib) when a slave dies from a wound inflicted by the master before the usurpation. The text presents two scenarios: (1) The master cuts off his slave's hand, then another person usurps the slave, and the slave dies from the cut. The usurper is liable to pay the value of the slave as one with a cut hand (i.e., the reduced value). (2) The master cuts the slave's hand while the slave is already in the hands of the usurper, and the slave dies from it. The master is deemed to have retaken possession, and the usurper is freed from liability. The commentator then discusses the reasoning: according to al-Hidayah, usurpation cuts off the transference of the injury (sirayah) because ownership changes, similar to a sale. However, this is contested. Imam Qadikhan argues that usurpation does not cut off transference unless the compensation (value) has become owed on the usurper by court order or consent, because transference is only cut off by change of ownership, and ownership only changes when the usurper becomes liable for the value. The text then cites a passage from Rahn al-Jami' al-Saghir which argues that the usurper is liable because the transference falls upon valuable property (the slave) in the usurper's possession, and the usurper's hand remains established. The author of al-Inayah objects that two legal hands cannot coexist on one thing, but the commentator rebuts that they can from different aspects: the master's hand is legal due to transference, the usurper's is legal due to actual possession. The ruling is that the usurper is liable for the value of the slave as a cut-hand slave in the first scenario, and not liable in the second. This is a critical refinement of the earlier ruling.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation, the wrongful taking of property without the owner's consent.
سراية (sirayah): Transference of an injury, where a wound spreads and causes death, attributing death to the original wound.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee, especially monetary compensation for damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position in this passage, when does transference (sirayah) become cut off in cases of ghasb?
A) Immediately upon usurpation.
B) Only when the usurper becomes liable for the value by court order or consent.
C) Never, because usurpation is a sin.
D) Only if the slave dies within 24 hours.

Key Takeaway
Usurpation does not automatically cut off the causal link of an injury; the usurper's liability is only triggered when compensation is owed, preserving the original injurer's responsibility.

Liability of the Usurper When a Master Cuts His Slave
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute on siraya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the usurper's liability in the two scenarios and explain the basis for the distinction, including the role of retaking possession.

Core Idea
A usurper is liable for the slave's value at usurpation; liability ends if the master retakes possession.

This passage presents two scenarios involving a master's cut on his slave followed by usurpation. The Hanafi ruling distinguishes based on whether the cut occurred before or after the usurper took possession. We will analyze the principle that ghasp necessitates guarantee and the intra-madhhab dispute about when ghasp cuts off the effect of a prior injury.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios and rulings
First scenario: master cuts slave's hand, then slave is usurped and dies from cut – usurper liable for value with cut. Second scenario: master cuts slave while in usurper's hand – usurper absolved. Note the decisive factor: the master's action in the second case is seen as retaking possession.
The timing of the cut relative to usurpation is critical.
2
Understand the principle: ghasp necessitates guarantee
The author states that ghasp (usurpation) creates liability for the thing usurped. In the first case, the usurper took the slave with an existing cut, so he guarantees the reduced value. In the second, the master's cutting after usurpation is treated as retaking, transferring liability back to the master.
This is a fundamental rule in Hanafi law of torts.
3
Examine the citation from al-Hidayah
Al-Hidayah distinguishes the cases by stating that ghasp cuts off siraya (the injurious effect) because ownership is like sale. In the first case, the cut is before, so the slave perishes by an 'heavenly affliction' after ghasp, making the usurper liable. In the second, the cut occurs during ghasp, so siraya attaches to the beginning – the master.
The analogy to sale is important: ownership transfer cuts off siraya.
4
Analyze Qadikhan's objection and the response
Qadikhan argues that ghasp does not cut off siraya until the usurper pays compensation, because ownership change requires payment. The author refutes by citing al-Jami' al-Saghir: siraya falls on valuable property, so the usurper is liable; ghasp is only removed by something stronger (compensation) or by the master's retaking.
The dispute is about whether ghasp alone can cut off siraya before compensation.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi position and the resolution of the dispute
Ibn Nujaym upholds the matn's ruling, rejecting Qadikhan's view. He argues that two hukmi hands (master's by siraya, usurper's by possession) can coexist from different perspectives, so the usurper remains liable until retaking or compensation.
The mufta bihi position is the ruling as stated in the matn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قطع يد عبده فغصبه رجل ومات منه ضمن قيمته اقطع وإن قطع يده في يد الغاصب فمات منه برئ لأن الغصب يوجب ضمان ما غصب ففي المسألة الأولى لما قطعه المولى نقصت قيمته بالقطع فيجب على الغاصب قيمته أقطع ، وفي الثانية حين قطع المولى العبد في يد الغاصب صار مسترداً له لاستيلائه عليه وبرئ الغاصب من ضمانه لوصول ملكه إلى يده. قال صاحب الهداية في الفرق بين المسألتين : إن الغصب قاطع للسراية لأن . الملك كالبيع فيصير كأنه هلك بآفة سماوية فتجب قيمته أقطع ولو لم يوجد القاطع في الفصل الثاني فكانت السراية مضافة إلى البداية فصار المولى متلفاً فيصير مسترداً وهذا مشكل، لأن السراية إنما تنقطع باعتبار تبدل الملك لاختلاف المستحقين والغصب ليس بسبب وضعاً والغاصب لا يملك إلا بأداء الضمان ضرورة كيلا يجتمع البدلان في ملك واحد وذلك بعد ملك المولى البدل ولم يوجد تحقيقه أن معنى قولهم بقطع السراية أن ما حصل من بالسراية يكون هدراً إلا أن تسبب ذلك إلى غير الجاني. واعترض عليه الإمام قاضيخان بأن هذا يخالف مذهبنا، فإن الغصب لا يقطع السراية ما لم يملك البدل على الغاصب بقضاء أو رضا لأن السراية أو رضا لأن السراية إنما تنقطع به باعتبار تبدل الملك، وإنما يتبدل الملك به إذا ملك البدل على الغاصب وهو قيمة العبد أقطع أما قبله فلا يضمن. وفي رهن الجامع الصغير في الباب الثاني من جناياته : إنما يضمن الغاصب هنا قيمة العبد لأن السراية وإن لم تنقطع بالغصب وردت على مال متقوم فوجب سبب الضمان فلا يبرأ عنه الغاصب إلا إذا ارتفع الغصب والشيء إنما يرتفع بما هو فوقه أو مثله ويد الغاصب ثابتة على المغصوب حقيقة وحكماً ويد المولى ثابتة عليه حكماً باعتبار السراية لا حقيقة لأن بعد الغصب لم تثبت يده على العبد حقيقة والثابت حكماً دون الثابت حقيقة وحكماً فلم يرتفع الغصب باتصال السراية فقصر عليه الضمان . قال صاحب العناية : فيه نظر لأنا لا نسلم أن يد الغاصب عليه ثابتة حكماً فإن يد المولى ثابتة عليه حكماً ولا يثبت على الشيء الواحد يدان حكميان بكمالهما. أقول نظره ساقط إذ لا وجه لمنع ثبوت يد الغاصب عليه حكماً فإن معنى ثبوت اليد على الشيء حكماً أن يترتب على تلك اليد حكماً من الأحكام وقد ترتب على يد الغاصب فيما نحن فيه وحوب الضمان ،بالإجماع، وأما يد منعه فليس بتام أيضاً إذ لا محذور في أن يثبت على الشيء الواحد يدان حكميان بكمالهما من جهتين مختلفتين وهنا كذلك فإن ثبوت يد المولى على العبد المغصوب منه حكماً باعتبار سراية القطع الذي صدر منه وثبوت يد الغاصب عليه حكماً باعتبار ثبوت يده عليه حقيقة فاختلفت الجهتان.
If a master cuts his slave's hand, then a man usurps him and he dies from it, the usurper guarantees his value with the cut. And if he cuts his hand while in the hand of the usurper and he dies from it, he (the usurper) is absolved. Because usurpation necessitates guarantee of what is usurped. In the first case, when the master cut, the value decreased by the cut, so the usurper must pay his value with the cut. In the second case, when the master cut the slave while in the usurper's hand, he (the master) becomes retaking him due to his control over him, and the usurper is absolved from guarantee due to the master's ownership reaching his hand. The author of al-Hidayah said in the distinction between the two cases: Usurpation cuts off the effect (of the injury), because ownership is like sale, so it becomes as if he perished by a heavenly affliction, so his value with the cut is obligatory. And if there were no cutter in the second case, then the effect is attributed to the beginning, so the master becomes a destroyer and thus becomes retaking. This is problematic, because the effect is only cut off by consideration of change of ownership due to difference of claimants, and usurpation is not a cause (of transfer) in terms of situation. And the usurper only owns by paying compensation necessarily so that two substitutes do not combine in one ownership, and that is after the master owns the substitute, which did not occur. The reality is that the meaning of their statement 'cutting off the effect' is that what occurs by way of effect is nullified unless that is caused to someone other than the injurer. Imam Qadikhan objected: This contradicts our madhhab, because usurpation does not cut off the effect as long as the substitute is not owned by the usurper by decree or consent. Because the effect is only cut off by change of ownership, and ownership only changes when he owns the substitute from the usurper, which is the value of the slave with the cut. Before that, he does not guarantee. And in Rahn al-Jami' al-Saghir in the second chapter of his crimes: The usurper only guarantees here the value of the slave because the effect, even if not cut off by usurpation, fell on valuable property, so the cause of guarantee became obligatory. So the usurper is not absolved unless the usurpation is removed. And a thing is only removed by what is above it or like it. The hand of the usurper is established on the usurped thing haqiqatan and hukman, and the hand of the master is established on it hukman by consideration of the effect, not haqiqatan, because after the usurpation, his hand is not established on the slave haqiqatan. And what is established hukman is less than what is established haqiqatan and hukman. So the usurpation is not removed by the attachment of the effect, so liability is restricted to him. The author of al-'Inayah said: This is debatable. We do not concede that the hand of the usurper is established on it hukman. The hand of the master is established on it hukman, and two complete hukmi hands cannot be established on a single thing. I say: His objection is baseless, because there is no reason to deny the establishment of the usurper's hand on it hukman. The meaning of establishment of hand on a thing hukman is that a ruling is attached to that hand, and indeed a ruling is attached to the usurper's hand in our case: the obligation of guarantee by consensus. As for his hand argument, it is also incomplete, because there is no harm in two complete hukmi hands being established on one thing from two different perspectives. And here it is so: the master's hand on the usurped slave is established hukman by consideration of the effect of the cut that he made, and the usurper's hand on it is established hukman by consideration of his hand being established on it haqiqatan, so the perspectives differ.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq (Ibn Nujaym) explains a matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq concerning two scenarios: (1) A master cuts his slave's hand, then someone usurps the slave, and the slave dies from the cut. The usurper must pay the value of the slave with the cut (i.e., the reduced value). (2) The master cuts the slave's hand while the slave is already in the hand of the usurper, and the slave dies. The usurper is absolved because the master's action in retaking possession nullifies the usurpation's liability. The key principle is that ghasp (usurpation) creates liability for the thing usurped. In the first case, the master's cut precedes ghasp, so the usurper is liable for the value as it is at the time of usurpation (with the cut). In the second, the cut occurs after the usurper's hand is established; the master's cutting action is seen as retaking, so the usurper's liability is transferred to the master. The commentator then discusses an objection by Qadikhan: The ruling in the first case that ghasp cuts off siraya (the injurious effect) contradicts the Hanafi principle that ghasp does not cut off siraya unless the usurper pays compensation. He cites a passage from Rahn al-Jami' al-Saghir to support that the usurper is liable because the effect falls on valuable property. The author of al-'Inayah objected that the usurper's hand cannot be hukmi when the master already has a hukmi hand. Ibn Nujaym refutes this by explaining that two hukmi hands can coexist from different perspectives: the master's by reason of the cut's effect, the usurper's by reason of his actual possession.

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasp): Taking another's property without right, with aggressive force or wrongful possession.
السراية (al-siraya): The effect of an injury that spreads or leads to death.
يد حكمية (yad hukmiyyah): A legal possession based on a legal rule, not physical control.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first scenario where the master cuts the slave's hand then the slave is usurped and dies, why is the usurper liable?
A) Because the usurper caused the cut.
B) Because the cut occurred after the usurpation.
C) Because the usurper is liable for the value at the time of usurpation, including the existing cut.
D) Because the master's cut is nullified by ghasp.

Key Takeaway
The usurper's liability depends on the sequence of injury and usurpation, and the master's retaking cuts off that liability.

Minor Usurps Another Minor's Slave: Liability
Ruling on liability of a minor for usurpation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a minor (mahjur) is liable for usurpation (ghasp) and explain the legal principle that tort liability attaches regardless of minority.

Core Idea
A minor's lack of contractual capacity does not shield him from tort liability.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding a minor (mahjur) who wrongfully takes another minor's slave. The key question: Is the minor liable? The answer clarifies a fundamental Hanafi principle about legal responsibility for tortious acts, even when the actor lacks capacity for contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their legal status
Both the usurper and the owner are mahjur (minors under legal interdiction). This means they legally are not fully competent to manage property or enter contracts, but they are subject to tort liability.
Mahjur refers to a person whose property is managed by a guardian due to minority or insanity.
2
Identify the wrongful act
The act is ghasp (usurpation) – wrongful taking of another's property without right. Here, the usurper takes a slave belonging to another mahjur.
Ghasp is a tort, not a contract.
3
Identify the outcome that triggers liability
The slave dies in the usurper's hand. This means the property is destroyed while in the wrongful possession of the usurper.
If the slave had been returned intact, liability might differ, but here destruction confirms liability.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle of liability
The ruling states: because the mahjur is held responsible for his actions (muakhkhadh bi-af'alihi), he is liable for this act of usurpation. Even though he lacks capacity for contracts, he is accountable for torts.
This principle distinguishes tort liability (daman) from contractual capacity.
5
State the final ruling
The usurping minor must compensate the value of the slave. The liability is a debt that may be paid from his property if he has assets; otherwise it remains until he becomes legally capable.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غصب محجور مثله فمات في يده ضمن) يعني إذا غصب عبد محجور عليه عبداً محجوراً عليه فمات المغصوب في يد الغاصب ضمنه لأن المحجور عليه مؤاخذ بأفعاله وهذا منها فيضمن.
If a mahjur (a minor under interdiction) usurps another like him and it (the property) dies in his hand, he is liable. That is, if a slave who is under interdiction usurps another slave under interdiction, and the usurped one dies in the hand of the usurper, he is liable for it because the one under interdiction is held responsible for his actions, and this is one of them, so he is liable.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, explained by the commentator. The ruling: If a minor who is subject to guardianship (mahjur) usurps another minor's slave, and the slave dies in the usurper's hand, the usurping minor is liable to pay its value. The reason is that a mahjur is legally accountable for his tortious acts. Although he may lack capacity for contracts, his liability for damage and usurpation is established. This is consistent with the Hanafi principle that the hand of a minor is not deficient in causing liability when he acts wrongfully.

Key Terms
محجور (mahjur): A person under legal interdiction due to minority or incapacity, whose property is managed by a guardian.
غصب (ghasp): Wrongful taking of another's property without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a minor (mahjur) liable when he usurps another minor's slave and it dies?
A) Because he is mature enough to know better.
B) Because liability for torts does not require capacity for contracts.
C) Because his guardian is liable instead.
D) Because the slave was valuable.

Key Takeaway
A minor under interdiction (mahjur) is liable for his tortious acts, including usurpation (ghasp), because liability for wrongful acts does not require contractual capacity.

Mudabbar's Two Crimes: Liability and Recovery for Master
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute on recovery for mudabbar's crimes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mudabbar's two crimes and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the master's recovery from the usurper.

Core Idea
A mudabbar's value is a single fund: master pays half to each victim, then recovers half from the usurper.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a nuanced scenario: a mudabbar (a slave whose freedom is deferred to after the master's death) commits two crimes, one while usurped by another person and one after being returned to the master. The master is liable for the slave's value, divided equally between the two victims, but then recovers half of that value from the usurper because half the liability arose from the usurper's transgression. The passage then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the master should give that recovered half to the first victim's guardian.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
List all actors: a usurper (ghasib), a mudabbar slave, the master of the mudabbar, and two victims (one from the first crime at the usurper's, one from the second crime after return).
The mudabbar status is key: the master cannot sell him or surrender him for crimes due to the tadbir.
2
State the master's primary liability
The master pays the full value of the mudabbar equally to the two victims (half each). The justification is that the master disabled himself from surrendering the slave by the prior tadbir, so he is like a free person ransoming after crimes.
The value is a single fund covering all crimes; it does not multiply with the number of crimes.
3
Explain the master's recourse against the usurper
The master recovers half of what he paid from the usurper because half the liability is attributable to the usurper's transgression (tghdi). The other half is due to the crime committed at the master's own place.
The recovery is for the portion of liability caused by the usurper's act of usurpation.
4
Map the intra-Hanafi dispute on distribution of the recovered half
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the master gives the recovered half to the first victim's guardian, arguing that the first victim's right to the entire value is prior in reality (haqiqatan). Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that the master does not give it to the first, because the recovery is a substitute (badal) for what he already paid the first; giving it would result in double recovery for the first victim.
Ibn Nujaym's commentary criticizes the answer of al-'Inayah (which supported Abu Hanifa) and suggests Muhammad's view is more consistent.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf, the first victim ultimately receives the full value (half from master, half from recovered amount). Under Muhammad, the first victim receives only the master's half, and the recovered half stays with the master (or is treated differently).
The text does not declare which is mufta bihi, but Ibn Nujaym seems to favor Muhammad's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مدبر جنى عند غاصبه ثم عند سيده ضمن قيمته لهما أي لو غصب رجل مدبراً فجنى عنده جناية ثم رده على مولاه فجنى عنده جناية أخرى ضمن المولى القيمة لولي الجنايتين فتكون بينهما نصفين لأن موجب جناية المدبر وإن كثرت قيمته واحدة فيجب ذلك على الملك للمولى لأنه هو الذي أعجز نفسه عن الدفع بالتدبير السابق من غير أن . مختاراً للفداء كما يصير في القن إذا أعتقه بعد الجنايات من غير أن يعلمها، وإنما كانت القيمة بينهما نصفين لاستوائهما في السبب . قال رحمه الله : ( ورجع بنصف قيمته على الغاصب أي رجع المولى بنصف ما ضمن من قيمة المدبر على الغاصب للتعدي لأنه ضمن القيمة بالجنايتين نصفها كان والنصف الآخر بسبب عنده فيرجع عليه لحقه من جهة الغاصب فصار كأنه لم يرد نصف بسبب يمتد العبد لأن رد المستحق بسبب وجد وعبده عند الغاصب كلا .رد قال رحمه الله : (ورده للأول) أي دفع المولى نصف القيمة الذي أخذه من الغاصب إلى ولي الجناية الأولى وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف . قالوا : ولهما أن حق الأول في جميع القيمة لأنه حين جنى في حقه لا يزاحمه أحد وإنما انتقص باعتبار مزاحمة الثاني إلى آخره. قال في العناية : واعترض بأن الثانية مقارنة للأولى حكماً فكيف يكون الحق للأول في جميع القيمة؟ وأجيب بأن المقارنة جعلت حكماً في حق الضمان لا غير والأولى مقدمة حقيقة وقد انعقدت موجبة لكل القيمة من غير مزاحمة وأمكن توفير موجبها فلا يمتنع بلا مانع أقول في الجواب بحث لأنا لا نسلم أن المقارنة جعلت حكماً في حق التضمين لا غير بل جعلت حكماً أيضاً في حق مشاركة ولي الجناية الثانية لولي الجناية الأولى كما أرشد إليه قول صاحب الهداية في الفصل السابق لأن الثانية مقارنة حكماً من وجه ولهذا يشارك ولي الجناية ا فإذا جعلت المقارنة حكماً في حق مشاركته وفي الجناية الثانية أيضاً كان ولي الجناية الثانية مزاحماً لولي الجناية الأولى في الاستحقاق جميع القيمة فكيف يأخذ ولي الجناية الأولى وحده كل القيمة مع مزاحمة الأولى الثانية له في استحقاقه إياه؟ وإن كان الاعتبار لتقدم الأولى حقيقة دون المقارنة الحكمية ينبغي أن لا يستحق ولي الثانية من قيمة المدبر وليس الأمر كذلك بالإجماع فليتأمل في جواب الشافعي. وقال محمد رحمه الله : لا يدفعها إليه لأن الذي يرجع به المولى على الغاصب عوض ما سلم لولي الجناية الأولى لأنه إنما يرجع على الغاصب فلا يدفع إليه كيلا يؤدي إلى اجتماع البدل والمبدل في ملك رجل، وكيلا يتكرر الاستحقاق. وقوله عوض ما سلم إلى ولي الجناية الأولى قلنا . كذلك هو شيئاً . هـ.
A mudabbar (a slave promised freedom after the master's death) commits a crime at the usurper's, then at his master's: The master guarantees his value to both (victims). That is, if a man usurps a mudabbar, and he commits a crime there, then he returns him to his master and he commits another crime there, the master guarantees the value to the two victims, so it is divided equally between them. Because the liability of a mudabbar's crime, even if many, is only one value. That is obligatory upon the owner for the master because he is the one who disabled himself from handing over by the prior tadbir (promise of freedom) without being free to choose ransom (fida'), as happens in the case of a qin (normal slave) whom he frees after crimes without knowing them. The value is divided equally because they are equal in the cause. He (the author) said: 'And he (the master) recovers half its value from the usurper.' That is, the master recovers half of what he paid as the value of the mudabbar from the usurper due to his transgression. Because he paid the value for the two crimes: half of it was (caused) by him, and the other half due to what was with him (the usurper). So he recovers from him his right from the side of the usurper, as if he did not pay half due to the ongoing cause of the slave, because the return of the due is by a cause that existed, and his slave was with the usurper as a non-return. He said: 'And he gives it to the first.' That is, the master gives the half value he took from the usurper to the first victim's guardian. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. They said: The right of the first is in all the value, because when he committed the crime in his right, no one rivals him; it is only reduced by the rivalry of the second etc. In al-'Inayah: It was objected that the second is equal to the first hukman, so how can the first have the right to all the value? It was answered: The equality (muqarana) is made hukman only in regard to guarantee, not other. The first is prior haqiqatan, and it (the crime) caused obligation of all the value without rivalry, and it is possible to fulfill its (the first's) cause without impediment. I say: There is investigation in the answer. We do not concede that the equality is made hukman only in regard to liability; rather it is also made hukman in regard to the participation of the second victim's guardian with the first, as indicated by the author of al-Hidayah in the previous section. Because the second is equal hukman in one respect, and therefore the second victim's guardian participates. So if equality is made hukman in regard to his participation, and in the second crime as well, then the second victim's guardian is a rival of the first in claiming all the value. So how can the first alone take all the value while the second rivals him in his entitlement? And if consideration is given to the first being prior haqiqatan without considering the hukmi equality, then the second should not deserve anything from the value of the mudabbar, but that is not the case by consensus. So let one reflect on the answer of al-Shafi'i. Muhammad (al-Shaybani) said: He does not give it to him, because what the master recovers from the usurper is compensation for what he delivered to the first victim's guardian. Since he only recovers from the usurper, he should not give it to him (the first) to avoid combining the substitute and the substituted in one ownership, and to avoid repetition of entitlement. And his statement: 'compensation for what he delivered to the first victim's guardian' we say: it is likewise something. End.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute. The scenario: a mudabbar (a slave whose freedom is deferred to after the master's death) is usurped. While with the usurper, he commits a crime (e.g., causing injury). Then he is returned to the master and commits another crime. The master is liable to pay the value of the mudabbar to the victims of the two crimes, divided equally. The reason: the mudabbar's value is a single amount covering all crimes, and the master is responsible because he disabled himself from surrendering the slave (due to the tadbir) without choosing to ransom. The master then recovers half of this amount from the usurper, because half is due to the usurper's transgression. The dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say the master gives the recovered half to the first victim's guardian. They argue the first victim has right to the entire value but is reduced by the second's claim. The author of al-'Inayah objects, arguing that the second crime is considered contemporaneous (muqarana) hukman, so the second victim shares equally. The author of the present work (Ibn Nujaym) criticizes the answer, asserting that the hukmi equality applies also to participation, so the second should share fully. He points out that Muhammad al-Shaybani holds a different view: the master does not give the half to the first; rather he keeps it or returns it to the usurper? Actually Muhammad says the master should not give it to the first victim's guardian because that would lead to combining compensation and the thing compensated (double recovery). The master's recovery from the usurper is compensation for what he paid to the first, so giving it to the first would result in the first getting double: the value and the recovery from the usurper. This is a nuanced dispute about the nature of the recovered amount.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose master has declared freedom after his death (tadbir).
المقارنة الحكمية (al-muqaranah al-hukmiyyah): Legal contemporaneity: two events are treated as simultaneous for legal purposes, even if not simultaneous in fact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, to whom does the master give the half he recovers from the usurper?
A) To the second victim's guardian.
B) To the first victim's guardian.
C) He keeps it.
D) He returns it to the usurper.

Key Takeaway
For a mudabbar's two crimes (one at usurper, one at master), the master pays the slave's value equally, recovers half from the usurper, and the dispute is whether to pass that half to the first victim or not.

Recourse Against the Usurper After Paying Blood Money for a Mudabbir Slave
Explains the master's right to recover from the usurper after paying compensation for the slave's crimes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the master's recourse against the usurper when a mudabbir slave commits multiple crimes, and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
The master recovers from the usurper only for crimes that occurred while the slave was in the usurper's possession.

The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a nuanced scenario: a mudabbir slave commits crimes both before and after being usurped. The master pays the full value of the slave to the victims, but can he recover from the usurper? The Hanafi masters differ on the details, revolving around the concept of 'combining substitute and substituted' in one ownership. This lesson unpacks the two scenarios and the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First scenario: mudabbir commits first crime at master's, then is usurped and commits second crime at usurper's. Second scenario (reverse): first crime at usurper's, then returned to master and commits second crime at master's.
The order of crimes determines the outcome of recourse.
2
Determine the master's total payment
In both scenarios, the master pays the full value of the mudabbir slave divided proportionally among the victims' guardians.
The value is paid because the mudabbir's crimes are the master's responsibility.
3
Trace the recourse claim in the first scenario
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the master recovers half the value (corresponding to the first crime) from the usurper because the first victim's right arose while the slave was in the usurper's possession.
Muhammad objects: he prevents payment to the first victim to avoid combining the substitute (value) and the substituted (slave) in one ownership.
4
Analyze the reverse scenario
In the reverse scenario, the master pays the full value but does not seek recourse a second time. The second crime occurred in his own possession, so no cause for recourse against the usurper exists.
Consensus among the three masters on no recourse in reverse scenario, though their reasons differ slightly.
5
Understand the underlying principle
The key principle is that recourse is allowed only when the cause of the victim's right (the crime) occurred in the usurper's hands. The prohibition of combining substitute and substituted in one ownership is a secondary consideration that creates the disagreement.
This principle applies to other similar cases of recourse against third parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ثم يرجع به على الغاصب أي يرجع المولى بذلك الذي دفعه إلى ولي الجناية الأولى ثانياً على الغاصب عندهما لأنه استحق من يده بسبب كان في يد الغاصب فيرجع عليه بذلك فصار كأنه لم يرد ولم يضمن له شيئاً إذا لم يبق شيء من العبد أو من بدله في يده. رحمه الله : (وبعكسه لا يرجع به ثانياً أي بعكس ما ذكره لا يرجع غاصب المولى على الغاصب بالقيمة ثانياً وصورته أن المدبر جنى عند مولاه أولاً فغصبه رجل فجنى عنده جناية أخرى ثم رده على المولى ضمن قيمته لولي الجنايتين فيكون بينهما نصفين، ثم يرجع المولى على الغاصب بنصف القيمة لأنه استحق عليه بسبب كان في يد الغاصب فيدفعه إلى ولي الجناية الأولى بالإجماع. أما عندهما فظاهر لما بينا، وأما عند محمد فإنه يمتنع الدفع إلى ولي الجناية الأولى في المسألة الأولى كيلا يجتمع البدل والمبدل في ملك واحد على ما بينا، وهنا لا يلزم ذلك لأن ما أخذه من الغاصب عوض ما دفع إلى ولي الجناية الثانية فإذا دفعه إلى ولي الأولى لا يجتمع البدلان في ملك واحد، وفي الأول يجتمع لأنه عوض ما أخذه هو بنفسه، ثم إذا دفعه إلى ولي الأولى لا يرجع به على الغاصب بالإجماع وهو المراد بقوله وبعكسه لا يرجع ثانياً لأن المولى لما لم يدفع ما أخذه من الغاصب إلى ولي الأولى سلم له ما أخذه من الغاصب فلم يتصور الرجوع عليه وهنا لم يسلم له بالإجماع ومع هذا لا يرجع على الغاصب بالإجماع بما دفعه ثانياً لأن الذي دفعه المولى إلى ولي الجناية الأولى ثانياً هنا بسبب جناية وجدت عنده فلا يرجع به على أحد بخلاف المسألة الأولى عندهما لأن دفع المولى ثانياً إلى ولي الجناية الأولى فيها بسبب جناية وجدت عند الغاصب فيرجع عليه هنا كما ذكرنا
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (Then he seeks recourse for it from the usurper), meaning the master seeks recourse for that which he paid to the guardian of the first crime a second time from the usurper according to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], because the guardian of the first crime had a right due from the hand of the master by reason of a cause that occurred while the slave was in the hand of the usurper, so he seeks recourse from him for that. Thus it is as if he did not return and did not guarantee anything for him when nothing of the slave or his substitute remains in his hand. May Allah have mercy on him: (And its reverse: he does not seek recourse a second time), meaning contrary to what he mentioned, the master of the mudabbir does not seek recourse from the usurper for the value a second time. Its scenario is: the mudabbir committed a crime while with his master first, then a man usurped him, and he committed another crime while with him, then he returned him to the master; the master guarantees his value to the guardians of the two crimes, so it is divided between them in halves. Then the master seeks recourse from the usurper for half the value because the guardian of the first crime had a right due from him by reason of a cause that occurred while in the hand of the usurper, so he pays it to the guardian of the first crime by consensus. As for according to them, it is apparent from what we explained. As for according to Muhammad, he refrains from paying to the guardian of the first crime in the first case to avoid combining the substitute and the substituted in one ownership, as we explained. Here, that does not apply because what he took from the usurper is compensation for what he paid to the guardian of the second crime; so when he pays it to the guardian of the first, the two substitutes do not combine in one ownership. In the first case, they combine because it is compensation for what he himself took. Then, when he pays it to the guardian of the first, he does not seek recourse from the usurper by consensus, and that is what is meant by his statement 'and its reverse: he does not seek recourse a second time' because when the master did not pay what he took from the usurper to the guardian of the first, what he took from the usurper becomes settled in his favor, so recourse is inconceivable. Here, it does not become settled in his favor by consensus, yet nonetheless he does not seek recourse from the usurper by consensus for what he paid a second time, because what the master paid to the guardian of the first crime a second time here is due to a crime that occurred while the slave was with him, so he does not seek recourse from anyone, unlike the first case according to them, because the master's payment a second time to the guardian of the first crime there was due to a crime that occurred while the slave was with the usurper, so he seeks recourse from him as we mentioned.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Raiq explaining the master's right to seek recourse against the usurper after paying blood money for crimes committed by a mudabbir slave. It covers two related scenarios. In the first scenario, a mudabbir commits a crime while with his master, then is usurped and commits another crime in the usurper's hands. The master pays the full value of the slave divided between the victims' heirs. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the master can then recover from the usurper half the value (the portion corresponding to the first crime) because that victim's right arose from the slave's possession by the usurper. Muhammad, however, prevents payment to the first victim in this scenario to avoid consolidating the substitute (value) and the substituted (slave) in one ownership. In the second scenario (the reverse), the mudabbir first commits a crime while usurped, then is returned to the master and commits another crime with him. Here, the master cannot recover from the usurper a second time because the second crime occurred while the slave was in his own hands. The text explains the exact legal reasoning (talil) and the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa (implied), Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated, but the commentary presents the views of the school's major authorities.

Key Terms
غاصب (ghāṣib): The person who unlawfully takes and holds the mudabbir slave.
مولى (mawlā): The master of the mudabbir slave; the owner whose manumission is deferred.
مدبر (mudabbir): A slave whose master has declared that he will be free upon the master's death.
ولي الجناية (walī al-jināyah): The guardian or heir of the victim who is entitled to receive blood money.
قيمة (qīmah): The monetary value of the slave at the time of the crime, paid by the master as compensation.
رجوع (rujū‘): Recourse: the master's right to claim from the usurper the amount he paid to the victims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He recovers half the value from the usurper
B) He recovers the full value from the usurper
C) He does not recover a second time from the usurper
D) He recovers only the portion for the first crime

Key Takeaway
The master's recourse against the usurper depends on where the cause of the victim's right arose; only the portion attributable to the usurper's possession can be recovered.
