Completing Fifty Oaths and Exemptions from Qasamah
Ruling on oath repetition and exemptions in qasamah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the method of repeating oaths to complete fifty in qasamah and identify the four categories exempted, including the intra-Hanafi difference regarding women.

Core Idea
Oaths in qasamah must reach fifty; repetition is wajib. Children, insane, women, and slaves are exempt due to lack of nasrah.

This passage addresses two procedural aspects of qasamah: how to achieve the required fifty oaths when the community is small, and which individuals are exempt from participating. The Hanafi school relies on textual proof and analogy to li'an to justify repetition. The exemptions are based on the concept of nasrah (ability to provide support). However, the commentator flags an important exception for women in certain contexts, highlighting a difference between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage presents two distinct rules: (1) repetition of oaths to complete fifty, and (2) exemptions from qasamah for four categories. Note the reasoning for each.
2
Analyze the repetition rule
If the number of eligible men is less than fifty, oaths are repeated on each man until the total reaches fifty. The justification is textual (the fifty-oath requirement) and analogical (li'an where one person repeats oaths).
The repetition is only for completing the count; if the count is already complete, no repetition is allowed.
3
List the exempted categories and their rationale
Children, insane persons, women, and slaves are exempt because they lack nasrah (support capability) or, for children and insane, the capacity for valid speech (qawl sahih).
The exemption is general but has an exception when the slain person is found in a village owned by a woman.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on women
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if a corpse is found in a woman's village, she is required to perform qasamah (with repeated oaths) and the 'aqila pays. Abu Yusuf places both qasamah and payment on the 'aqila.
This shows that the absolute exemption for women is not without qualification.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a hypothetical case, state who must swear, how many oaths each, and whether any exemptions apply, citing the relevant parts of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كرر الحلف عليهم ليتم خمسين يميناً ولا قسامة على صبي ومجنون وامرأة وعبد ولا قسامة ولا دية في ميت لا أثر به أو يسيل دم من فمه أو أنفه أو دبره بخلاف عينه
لأنه ليس فيه نص فلو أوجبناهما لأوجبناهما بالقياس وهو ممتنع ، ثم إن حلف برئ، وإن نكل ففي دعوى المال يثبت وفي دعوى القصاص فهو على الاختلاف الذي ذكرناه في كتاب الدعوى. قال رحمه الله : ( وإن لم يتم العدد كرر الحلف عليهم ليتم خمسين يميناً) لأن الخمسين وجبت بالنصف فيجب تمامه ما أمكن ولا يشترط فيه الوقوف على الفائدة فيما يثبت بالنص وقد روي عن عمر رضي الله عنه أن قضى بالدية وروي عن شريح والنخعي مثل ذلك ولأن فيه استعظاماً لأمر الدم فيتكمل، وتكرار اليمين من واحد على سبيل الوجوب ممكن شرعاً كما في كلمات اللعان، وإن كان العدد كاملاً فأراد الولي أن يكرر على أحدهم فليس له ذلك لأن المصير إلى التكرار ضرورة الإكمال وقد كمل . قال رحمه الله: (ولا قسامة على صبي ومجنون وامرأة وعبد) لأنهم ليسوا من أهل النصرة وإنما هو أتباع والنصرة لا تقوم بالاتباع واليمين على أهل النصرة، ولأن الصبي والمجنون ليسا من أهل القول الصحيح واليمين قول قوله وامرأة وعبد لأنهما ليسا من أهل النصرة واليمين على أهلها . أقول : يشكل إطلاق هذا بقول أبي حنيفة ومحمد في مسألة وهي أنه لو وجد قتيل في قرية لامرأة فعند أبي حينفة ومحمد عليها القسامة تكرر عليها الأيمان والدية على عاقلتها، وأما عند أبي يوسف القسامة أيضاً على العاقلة .
The oath is repeated upon them to complete fifty oaths. And no qasamah on a child, insane person, woman, or slave. And no qasamah and no blood money for a dead person with no trace [of violence] or if blood flows from his mouth, nose, or anus, unlike his eye [or ear]. Because there is no text regarding it; if we made it obligatory, we would have made it obligatory by analogy, and that is impermissible. Then if he swears, he is clear; if he refuses, then in a claim of property it is established, and in a claim of qisas it is according to the difference we mentioned in the Book of Claims. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'And if the number is not complete, the oath is repeated upon them to complete fifty oaths' because the fifty were made obligatory [textually], so its completion is obligatory as much as possible. It is not conditioned upon pausing at benefit in what is established by text. It has been related from Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that he decided the blood money, and from Shuraih and al-Nakha'i similarly. And because that includes magnifying the matter of blood, so it is completed. And repetition of the oath from one person as an obligation is possible legally, as in the words of li'an. And if the number is complete, and the heir wants to repeat on one of them, he does not have that, because resorting to repetition is a necessity for completion, and it has already been completed. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'And no qasamah on a child, insane person, woman, or slave' because they are not people of support (nasrah); they are only followers, and support does not stand by followers. The oath is upon people of support. And because the child and insane person are not people of correct speech, and the oath is speech. And the woman and slave because they are not people of support, and the oath is upon its people. I say: This general application is problematic because of the statement of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad in an issue: if a slain person is found in a village belonging to a woman, then according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, qasamah is upon her, repeated upon her oaths, and the blood money is upon her supporting clan ('aqila). According to Abu Yusuf, the qasamah also is upon the 'aqila.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with subsequent commentary on two issues: (1) repetition of oaths to complete fifty, and (2) exemptions from qasamah. The matn states that if there are fewer than fifty men, the oath is repeated upon them until fifty are reached. The commentator justifies this by noting the textual obligation of fifty oaths (based on the hadith of the Prophet) and analogizing to the repetition in li'an (mutual cursing). If the required number is complete, the heir cannot demand repetition from a single person. The second ruling excludes children, insane persons, women, and slaves from performing qasamah because they are not considered 'people of support' (ahl al-nasrah) who can defend the locality. The oath is meant for those who can effectively bear the responsibility. However, the commentator notes a potential inconsistency: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, in the case of a corpse found in a village owned by a woman, place qasamah on her (with repetitive oaths) and the blood money on her 'aqila, but Abu Yusuf places the qasamah on the 'aqila as well. This shows that the absolute exemption for women is not without qualification; when there is no male inhabitant, a female may be required to swear.

Key Terms
نصرة (nasrah): The ability to provide support or defense, particularly in the context of collective responsibility for blood money.
عاقلة (aqila): The male paternal relatives of the killer who are obliged to pay blood money on his behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because they are not legally responsible (mukallaf)
B) Because they lack the capacity for correct speech
C) Because they are not considered people of support (nasrah)
D) Because qasamah is only for men

Key Takeaway
Oaths in qasamah must total fifty, achieved by repetition if necessary; children, insane persons, women, and slaves are generally exempt due to lack of nasrah, but the exemption for women is qualified when the village is her property.

Indications of Violent Death: Blood from Eye or Ear Triggers Qasamah
Specifies when a dead body with no visible injury or blood from certain orifices incurs no qasamah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when blood discharge from a corpse indicates violent death and triggers qasamah, and distinguish between natural and violent signs.

Core Idea
Blood from eye or ear indicates beating; blood from mouth, nose, anus indicates natural death.

In Hanafi fiqh, not every dead body triggers qasamah on the locality. The key is evidence that the person was killed. This lesson explains how the location of blood discharge helps determine whether death was natural or violent, and what legal consequences follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Recognize the scenario: a corpse is found with no visible injuries or with blood flowing from an orifice.
2
Classify the blood source
Determine whether the blood came from mouth, nose, anus (natural) or from eye, ear (unusual).
Natural orifices indicate natural death (hatf anfih).
3
Apply the ruling
If natural: no qasamah, no diyah. If violent (eye/ear): qasamah and diyah are wajib.
The rationale is that unusual bleeding implies beating.
4
Understand the qiyas
The asl (mouth/nose/anus) is natural death, hukm: no liability. The illa is that blood from those places is normal. The far' (eye/ear) has the opposite illa (abnormal), so opposite hukm.
This is qiyas jali (clear analogy).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا قسامة ولا دية في ميت لا أثر به أو يسيل دم من فمه أو أنفه أو دبره بخلاف عينه وأذنه لأن القسامة تجب في القتيل وهذا ليس بقتيل وإنما مات حتف أنفه وفي مثله لا قسامة ولا غرامة لأن الغرامة تتبع فعل العبد والقسامة لاحتمال القتل منهم فلا بد من أثر يكون بالميت يستدل به على أنه قتيل بخلاف ما إذا خرج دمه من عينه وأذنه لأنه لا يخرج عادة إلا من كثرة الضرب فيكون قتيلاً ظاهراً فتجري عليه أحكامه وهو المراد بة وله بخلاف عينه وأذنه. ولو وجد بدن القتيل كله أو أكثر من نصفه أو النصف ومعه الرأس في ملحة فعلى أهلها القسامة ،والدية وإن وجد نصفه مشقوقاً بالطول أو وجد أقل من النصف وكان معه الرأس أو لم يكن فلا شيء عليهم لأن هذا حكم عرف بالنص وقد ورد به في البدن ولكن للأكثر حكم الكل فأجرينا عليه أحكامه تعظيماً للآدمي والأقل ليس معناه فلا يلحق به وإلا لو اعتبرناه لاجتمعت الديات والقسامات بمقابلة شخص واحد بأن توجد أطرافه في القرى مفرقة وهو غير مشروع، وينبني على هذا صلاة الجنازة لأنها لا تتكرر كالقسامة والدية. قال الشارح ولو وجد فيهم جنين أو سقط ليس به أثر الضرب لا شيء على أهل المحلة لأنه لا يفوق الكبير حالاً، وإن كان به أثر الضرب وهو تام الخلق وجبت القسامة والدية عليهم لأن الظاهر أن تام الخلف ينفصل حياً إلى آخره. أقول: في تحرير هذه المسألة فتور من وجوه الأول أن الجنين على ما صرحوا به في عامة كتب اللغة الولد ما دام
And no qasamah and no blood money for a dead person with no trace [of violence] or if blood flows from his mouth, nose, or anus, unlike his eye and ear because qasamah is obligatory for a slain person, and this is not a slain person; he died of natural causes. In such a case, there is no qasamah and no liability because liability follows the act of a person, and qasamah is for the possibility that they killed him. So there must be a trace on the deceased indicating that he is a slain person, unlike when blood comes out of his eye or ear because it does not usually come out except from heavy beating, so he is evidently a slain person, and its rulings apply. And if the entire body of the slain person is found, or more than half, or half with the head in a locality, then upon its people is qasamah and blood money. And if half of it is found split lengthwise, or less than half with the head or without, then there is nothing upon them because this ruling is known by text, and it has been reported concerning the body. But the majority has the ruling of the whole, so we apply its rulings to honor the human being. The lesser is not like that, so it is not annexed; otherwise if we considered it, multiple blood monies and qasamahs would be collected in exchange for a single person if his limbs are found scattered in villages, which is not legislated. Upon this is based the funeral prayer, because it is not repeated like qasamah and blood money. The commentator said: And if a fetus or stillborn is found among them with no trace of beating, there is nothing upon the inhabitants because it does not surpass the adult in status. And if it has a trace of beating and is fully developed, qasamah and blood money are obligatory upon them because the apparent is that a fully developed fetus separates alive until the end. I say: In refining this issue, there is weakness in several respects: first, the fetus, according to what they stated in most language books, is the child as long as...
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with commentary on when a corpse does or does not incur qasamah and blood money. The matn states that no qasamah or blood money is due for a corpse that has no visible injury or if blood flows from the mouth, nose, or anus. However, if blood flows from the eye or ear, it indicates beating, so the corpse is treated as a slain person. The commentator explains the rationale: qasamah requires the victim to be clearly slain (qatil), not one who died naturally. Blood from eyes or ears is abnormal and suggests violence. Next, the ruling: if the entire body or more than half, or half with the head, is found in a locality, the inhabitants are liable. If only half lengthwise or less than half is found (with or without head), there is no liability. This is based on the prophetic practice. The majority of a body is treated as the whole, to honor human dignity. The same principle applies to funeral prayer: it is not repeated for scattered parts. Finally, a comment on a fetus: if no trace of beating and not fully developed, no liability; if fully developed and with traces, qasamah and blood money are due. The commentator notes some issues in the details of the fetus ruling, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
حتف أنفه (hatf anfih): Natural death, dying in one's bed without external cause.
قسامة (qasamah): Collective oath taken by the locality to deny involvement, which if refused, incurs blood money.
دية (diyah): Blood money paid to the victim's family.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, which of the following blood discharges from a corpse would trigger qasamah and diyah?
A) Blood from the mouth
B) Blood from the nose
C) Blood from the anus
D) Blood from the ear

Key Takeaway
The source of bleeding determines whether a corpse is considered slain: eye/ear blood incurs qasamah; mouth/nose/anus blood does not.

Body Parts and Fetus: Qasamah for Incomplete Remains
Explains qasamah liability for incomplete body parts and the fetus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on qasamah when only parts of a body are found, and state the ruling for a fetus with signs of beating.

Core Idea
More than half a body with the head triggers qasamah; a fully developed fetus with beating signs does too.

When a body is found incomplete, the law must define when the locality is liable. This lesson explains the thresholds based on prophetic practice and the honor due to humans. It also covers the unique case of a fetus.

Guided Path
1
List the scenarios
Whole body, more than half, half with head, half split lengthwise, less than half with/without head, and a fetus.
2
Apply the text for each
Whole body, more than half, half with head: qasamah and diyah wajib. Half split lengthwise or less than half: nothing.
The ruling is based on prophetic practice and the principle that the majority has the ruling of the whole.
3
Understand the rationale
Treating the majority as the whole honors the human being and prevents multiple claims from scattered parts.
This is also applied to funeral prayer (not repeated).
4
Analyze the fetus case
Fetus with no trace of beating and not fully developed: no liability. If fully developed (tamm al-khalq) and has beating signs: qasamah and diyah wajib.
The commentator notes some weakness in the fetus ruling but the text establishes the basic rule.

Completing Fifty Oaths in Qasamah by Repetition
Commentary on the ruling of repeating oaths in qasamah when the number of heirs is incomplete

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on repetition of oaths in qasamah when the number of heirs is less than fifty, and explain the evidence from the Sunnah and analogy.

Core Idea
If the number of eligible oath-takers is less than fifty, they must repeat oaths to complete fifty, because the obligation of fifty oaths is established by text and must be fulfilled as far as possible.

In qasamah, the heirs of a victim must swear fifty oaths to establish liability. What happens if there are fewer than fifty eligible male heirs? This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq, commented upon by Ibn Nujaym, clarifies that the oaths must be repeated by the available individuals to reach the required number. The ruling is supported by the actions of Caliph Umar and earlier jurists, and by analogy with li'an (mutual cursing oaths between spouses).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The matn states: if the number of eligible oath-takers (heirs) is less than fifty, the oaths are repeated upon them to complete fifty oaths.
The ruling is that repetition is wajib.
2
Locate the dalil
The commentator cites the actions of Umar, Shuraih, and al-Nakha'i as evidence (Sunnah of the Companions). Also, analogy is drawn with li'an, where oaths are repeated.
The dalil is from Sunnah ahad, with zanni thubut and dalala.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The actions of Umar show that the fifty oaths are a binding requirement that must be completed, even if it requires repetition. The analogy with li'an demonstrates that repetition by a single person is legally possible.
The wajh is that the text establishes the number, and repetition is a means to fulfill it.
4
Note the condition
If the number of heirs is complete (fifty or more), the guardian cannot force any single person to repeat oaths, because the need for repetition disappears.
The condition is that repetition is only for necessity of completion.
5
Apply the principle
The obligation of fifty oaths is wajib by nass. Obligations must be fulfilled as much as possible. Therefore, when the number of oath-takers is insufficient, repetition is required to achieve the prescribed number.
This principle is a manifestation of 'idha wajaba al-shay' wajaba kullu ma la yatimmu illa bihi'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وإن لم يتم العدد كرر الحلف عليهم ليتم خمسين يميناً) لأن الخمسين وجبت بالنصف فيجب تمامه ما أمكن ولا يشترط فيه الوقوف على الفائدة فيما يثبت بالنص وقد روي عن عمر رضي الله عنه أن قضى بالدية وروي عن شريح والنخعي مثل ذلك ولأن فيه استعظاماً لأمر الدم فيتكمل، وتكرار اليمين من واحد على سبيل الوجوب ممكن شرعاً كما في كلمات اللعان، وإن كان العدد كاملاً فأراد الولي أن يكرر على أحدهم فليس له ذلك لأن المصير إلى التكرار ضرورة الإكمال وقد كمل .
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: '(And if the number is not complete, the oath is repeated upon them to complete fifty oaths)' because the fifty were obligated by the text, so its completion is obligatory as much as possible, and it is not conditional upon stopping at benefit regarding what is established by the text. It has been narrated from Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, that he judged by the diyah, and similar has been narrated from Shuraih and al-Nakha'i. And because it contains magnification of the matter of blood, so it is completed. And repetition of the oath from one person by way of obligation is possible legally, just as in the words of li'an. And if the number is complete, and the guardian wants to repeat upon one of them, he does not have that, because resorting to repetition is a necessity of completion, and it has been completed.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling on qasamah when the number of eligible oath-takers (the adult male heirs of the victim's family) is less than fifty. The matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq states that the oaths should be repeated by the available individuals to make up fifty oaths. The commentator gives the reasoning: the obligation of fifty oaths is established by the text (nass) from the Prophet's practice, so it must be completed as far as possible. He cites the actions of Caliph Umar and earlier jurists Shuraih and al-Nakha'i. The repetition of oaths by an individual is permissible by analogy with the repeated oaths in li'an (mutual cursing between spouses). However, if the full number of fifty persons is available, the guardian (wali) cannot force any one person to repeat oaths, because the need for repetition only arises due to the necessity of completing the count.

Key Terms
القَسامة (al-qasāmah): An oath taken fifty times by the heirs of a victim in cases of suspected homicide where there is evidence but no direct witness.
اللعان (al-li‘ān): Mutual cursing oaths between spouses accusing each other of adultery, involving repeated oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must happen if the number of eligible oath-takers (heirs) is less than fifty in a qasamah case?
A) The case is dismissed.
B) The available individuals repeat oaths until fifty oaths are completed.
C) The defendant is automatically liable.
D) The number is reduced to twenty-five.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qasamah, the obligation of fifty oaths must be completed even if it requires repetition by fewer heirs, based on the text and precedent.

Persons Excluded from Qasamah: Minor, Insane, Woman, Slave
Commentary on the ruling that qasamah is not upon minors, insane persons, women, or slaves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories excluded from qasamah and explain the rationale based on nusrah and correct speech.

Core Idea
Qasamah is only for those who can provide protection (nusrah) and give valid oaths – minors, the insane, women, and slaves do not qualify.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains why the Hanafi school exempts four specific groups from the obligation of qasamah: minors, the insane, women, and slaves. The key concept is 'nusrah' (ability to provide aid and protection), which is a prerequisite for the oath. The commentator also highlights that minors and the insane lack the capacity for valid speech, which is essential for taking an oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The author states that qasamah is not upon minors, insane persons, women, or slaves. This is the base ruling from the matn.
The ruling is a negation of obligation (hukm wad'i).
2
Understand the condition
Qasamah is only required from 'ahl al-nusrah' – people who can provide protection and aid to the victim's family. This is the underlying condition for the obligation.
Nusrah is a social and legal capacity.
3
Apply the condition to each group
Minors and insane persons lack nusrah and also lack correct speech (qawl sahih), which is needed for the oath. Women and slaves are considered followers (tabi'), not protectors, so they are not from ahl al-nusrah.
Each group fails the condition, so the ruling applies.
4
Conclude the hukm
Therefore, none of these four categories are liable for qasamah. The exemption is based on their inherent attributes, not on individual circumstances.
This is consistent with Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (ولا قسامة على صبي ومجنون وامرأة وعبد) لأنهم ليسوا من أهل النصرة وإنما هو أتباع والنصرة لا تقوم بالاتباع واليمين على أهل النصرة، ولأن الصبي والمجنون ليسا من أهل القول الصحيح واليمين قول قوله وامرأة وعبد لأنهما ليسا من أهل النصرة واليمين على أهلها .
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: '(And there is no qasamah upon a minor, an insane person, a woman, or a slave)' because they are not from the people of protection (ahl al-nusrah); they are only followers, and protection is not established by following. And the oath is upon the people of protection. And because the minor and the insane are not among those with correct speech, and the oath is a saying. And the woman and slave are not from the people of protection, and the oath is upon them.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling that qasamah is not required from minors, insane persons, women, or slaves. The rationale is that qasamah is an oath taken by those who can provide 'nusrah' (protection/aid) to the victim's family. These four categories are either not legally capable (minors, insane) or are considered followers rather than protectors (women, slaves). The oath is a form of speech, and minors and insane persons lack valid speech. Thus, they are excluded from the obligation of qasamah.

Key Terms
النصرة (al-nuṣrah): The ability to provide protection or aid to the victim's family, typically associated with adult free males.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They cannot afford the diyah.
B) They are not from ahl al-nusrah and lack correct speech.
C) They are not relatives of the victim.
D) Their oath is not considered valid in any case.

Key Takeaway
Four categories are excluded from qasamah because they lack nusrah or valid speech, ensuring the oath is only upon those capable of communal protection.

Qasamah on Women: The Exceptional Case of a Woman's Village
Commentary noting a conflict between the general rule and a specific case regarding women in their own village

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf regarding a woman's qasamah when a body is found in her village, and explain the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between the two rulings.

Core Idea
A woman's ownership of the village can trigger qasamah on her, but Abu Yusuf keeps it on the 'aqilah.

In Hanafi law, qasamah (repeated oaths) is generally not required of women. However, an exceptional case arises when a dead body is discovered in a village owned by a woman. The early Hanafi masters differed on whether the woman herself must swear the oaths or whether the responsibility shifts entirely to her 'aqilah. This lesson examines the intra-Hanafi dispute and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Recall the default Hanafi position that qasamah is not on women. This is the established ruling in most cases.
2
Present the exceptional scenario
A dead body is found in a village owned by a woman. This particular context creates a conflict with the general rule.
The exception is based on ownership (lawth).
3
State the positions
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: the woman must perform qasamah and the diyah is on her 'aqilah. Abu Yusuf: the qasamah is on the 'aqilah, not on the woman directly.
Both are Hanafi positions.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: who swears the oaths? Under the first view, the woman herself must take the fifty oaths; under the second, the 'aqilah bears that burden.
The diyah payment is on the 'aqilah in both views.
5
Reflect on the critical refinement
This case shows that general rules in fiqh may have exceptions based on context. The author presents this as a problem with the general ruling, demonstrating the need for careful application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : يشكل إطلاق هذا بقول أبي حنيفة ومحمد في مسألة وهي أنه لو وجد قتيل في قرية لامرأة فعند أبي حينفة ومحمد عليها القسامة تكرر عليها الأيمان والدية على عاقلتها، وأما عند أبي يوسف القسامة أيضاً على العاقلة .
I say: The generality of this is problematic with the statement of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on an issue: that if a dead body is found in a village belonging to a woman, then according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the qasamah is upon her, the oaths are repeated upon her, and the diyah is upon her 'aqilah. But according to Abu Yusuf, the qasamah is also upon the 'aqilah.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This is the author's own comment (qultu) questioning the general rule that no qasamah is on women. He points out a specific case: if a dead body is found in a village owned by a woman, then Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule that she must perform qasamah (the oaths) and the diyah is on her 'aqilah (the clan responsible for paying blood money). Abu Yusuf, however, says that qasamah is also on the 'aqilah, not on the woman directly. This shows an intra-Hanafi dispute: in general women are excluded, but in this specific scenario, the woman is responsible because the body is found on her property.

Key Terms
العاقلة (al-‘āqilah): The paternal relatives (clan) responsible for paying diyah on behalf of the killer.
القسامة (al-qasāmah): The fifty oaths repeated in cases of suspected homicide to determine liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The woman is exempt from qasamah.
B) The woman must perform qasamah and her 'aqilah pays diyah.
C) The woman pays diyah but does not swear oaths.
D) The case is dismissed.

Key Takeaway
Ownership of the locus of a crime can create an exception to the general rule exempting women from qasamah, highlighting an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

No Qasamah for Corpse Without Signs of Violence
Commentary on the ruling that no qasamah or diyah is due for a corpse without signs of violence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qasamah when a corpse shows no signs of violence, explain the distinction between blood from natural orifices versus eye/ear, and apply the principle to new scenarios.

Core Idea
Without signs of violence, a corpse is presumed natural death — no qasamah or diyah, unless blood flows from eye or ear, which indicates beating.

In the Hanafi school, qasamah and diyah are only imposed when there is a reasonable presumption that the deceased was killed by someone. This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that the absence of visible marks of violence, or the presence of blood from orifices that could indicate natural causes (mouth, nose, anus), negates that presumption. However, blood from the eye or ear is deemed unnatural and thus triggers the full rulings. This distinction is essential for correctly applying the laws of diyat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The matn states that there is no qasamah and no diyah for a corpse that has no visible injury or from whose mouth, nose, or anus blood flows. Identify this as the default ruling.
The ruling is a negation of obligation.
2
Recognize the exception
The exception is when blood flows from the eye or ear. In that case, qasamah and diyah are due. Note that the passage explicitly contrasts these two categories.
Blood from eye/ear is considered an indication of beating.
3
Analyze the reasoning (talil)
The author provides the reasoning: qasamah is for a 'slain' person. Without signs of violence, the presumption is natural death. Blood from mouth/nose/anus can occur naturally, but blood from eye/ear is almost always from severe beating.
This is a rational proof based on customary occurrence (ghalabah).
4
Apply to a hypothetical
Consider a corpse found with a bruise on the arm. Would qasamah be due? The passage requires an 'effect' that indicates slaying. A bruise alone may not be enough; it must be such that it implies killing. But the passage specifically mentions blood from eye/ear as definitive. Apply the principle consistently.
The Hanafi school requires a clear indication of violence for qasamah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (ولا قسامة ولا دية في ميت لا أثر به أو يسيل دم من فمه أو أنفه أو دبره بخلاف عينه وأذنه لأن القسامة تجب في القتيل وهذا ليس بقتيل وإنما مات حتف أنفه وفي مثله لا قسامة ولا غرامة لأن الغرامة تتبع فعل العبد والقسامة لاحتمال القتل منهم فلا بد من أثر يكون بالميت يستدل به على أنه قتيل بخلاف ما إذا خرج دمه من عينه وأذنه لأنه لا يخرج عادة إلا من كثرة الضرب فيكون قتيلاً ظاهراً فتجري عليه أحكامه وهو المراد بة وله بخلاف عينه وأذنه.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: '(And there is no qasamah and no diyah for a deceased who has no effect [of violence] upon him, or from whose mouth, nose, or anus blood flows, contrary to his eye and ear)' because qasamah is obligatory for the slain person, and this is not slain; rather he died naturally. In such a case, there is no qasamah and no compensation, because compensation follows the act of the servant, and qasamah is due to the possibility of killing by them. So there must be an effect upon the deceased by which it is inferred that he was slain. Contrary to when blood comes out of his eye or ear, because it usually comes out only from severe beating, so he is apparently slain, so its rulings apply to him. That is what is intended by 'and his eye and ear'.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for qasamah to apply. If a corpse is found with no visible marks of violence, or if blood is flowing from the mouth, nose, or anus (which could be natural causes), then no qasamah or diyah is imposed. The reasoning is that qasamah and financial compensation (diyah) are tied to the probability that the person was killed by someone. Without such signs, the presumption is natural death. However, if blood flows from the eye or ear, it is presumed to be from beating (as that is not natural), so qasamah and diyah are due. The author clarifies that the exemption is only for the three orifices (mouth, nose, anus), not the eye or ear.

Key Terms
حتف أنفه (ḥatf anfih): Natural death (literally 'death of his nose'), i.e., death not caused by violence.
قسامة (qasāmah): An oath sworn by the community or plaintiffs in cases of suspected murder when there is insufficient evidence; also the resulting liability.
دية (diyah): Blood money, financial compensation paid to the heirs of a homicide victim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a corpse is found with blood flowing from its mouth, what is the ruling?
A) Qasamah and diyah are obligatory.
B) No qasamah or diyah is due.
C) Only diyah is due.
D) The case requires further investigation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school strictly ties qasamah and diyah to visible signs of violence, with blood from the eye or ear being the only natural-orifice exception.

The Majority Rule for Partial Body and Qasamah
Ruling on when partial remains trigger qasamah and diyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for imposing qasamah and diyah when a partial body is found, and explain the legal reasoning based on majority and preventing multiple claims.

Core Idea
For qasamah and diyah, a partial body must be a majority (half with head or more); less than half triggers no blood claim.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Hanafi fiqh: when a slain person's body is only partially found, what triggers the collective obligation of qasamah and diyah on the locality? The passage establishes a clear threshold based on the majority of the body, drawing on textual authority and practical reasoning to avoid multiple claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is finding a slain person's body (whole or partial) in a locality. The ruling: the people of that locality must perform qasamah and pay diyah.
Qasamah is a collective oath taken by the locality to deny responsibility; diyah is blood money.
2
Note the conditions for obligation
The obligation is conditional on finding the entire body, or more than half, or half with the head. If half is found split lengthwise, or less than half (with or without head), no obligation arises.
The head is considered significant; half with head qualifies as majority.
3
Understand the proof (dalil)
The ruling is based on a transmitted text (nass) that treats the majority of the body as the whole. The author states 'this is a ruling known by text'.
The exact text is not quoted but assumed from Hadith or early practice.
4
Understand the rationale (ta'lil)
Two reasons: (1) honoring human dignity (ta'zim al-adami) by treating the majority as the whole; (2) preventing multiple claims—if any part triggered a claim, scattered limbs would create multiple diyahs and qasamahs for one person.
This is a form of istihsan (juristic preference) based on maqasid.
5
Apply to funeral prayer (janazah)
The same principle applies: the funeral prayer is not repeated for parts found separately, just as qasamah is not repeated.
This shows consistency in treating the majority as the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وجد بدن القتيل كله أو أكثر من نصفه أو النصف ومعه الرأس في ملحة فعلى أهلها القسامة ،والدية وإن وجد نصفه مشقوقاً بالطول أو وجد أقل من النصف وكان معه الرأس أو لم يكن فلا شيء عليهم لأن هذا حكم عرف بالنص وقد ورد به في البدن ولكن للأكثر حكم الكل فأجرينا عليه أحكامه تعظيماً للآدمي والأقل ليس معناه فلا يلحق به وإلا لو اعتبرناه لاجتمعت الديات والقسامات بمقابلة شخص واحد بأن توجد أطرافه في القرى مفرقة وهو غير مشروع، وينبني على هذا صلاة الجنازة لأنها لا تتكرر كالقسامة والدية.
And if the entire body of the slain person is found, or more than half of it, or half with the head, in a locality, then upon its people is qasamah and diyah. And if half of it is found split lengthwise, or less than half is found with or without the head, then nothing is upon them. Because this is a ruling known by text and has been reported regarding the body. But the majority has the ruling of the whole, so we applied its rulings upon it, magnifying the human being. And the lesser [portion] does not have its meaning, so it is not attached to it. Otherwise, if we considered it, multiple diyahs and qasamahs would gather for a single person if his limbs were found scattered in different villages, and that is not legislated. And the funeral prayer is built upon this, because it is not repeated like qasamah and diyah.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario when only part of a body is found. According to the Hanafi school, if the entire body is found, or more than half, or half with the head, in a particular locality, the people of that locality are subject to qasamah and diyah. If half is found split lengthwise (implying it is not intact), or less than half with or without the head, then no claim lies. The reasoning is based on reported text (nass) that the 'body' refers to the majority. The majority is treated as the whole for the sake of honoring human dignity. If less than half were accepted, it could lead to multiple claims for different parts in different villages, which is not permissible. This principle also applies to the funeral prayer (janazah), which is not repeated for parts of a body.

Key Terms
ملحة (millaḥah): A locality, village, or district; the place where the body is found.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If half of a dead body is found split lengthwise, what is the ruling?
A) Qasamah and diyah are obligatory.
B) No qasamah or diyah is due.
C) Only diyah is due.
D) The funeral prayer is not performed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school imposes qasamah and diyah on a locality only when the majority of the body (more than half or half with head) is found there, to honor human dignity and prevent multiple claims.

Ruling on Qasamah and Diyah for a Fetus with Signs of Beating
Ruling on qasamah and diyah for a fetus, with critique and usul debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a fully formed fetus is found dead with signs of beating, and explain the role of the apparent state (zahir) in establishing liability.

Core Idea
A fully formed fetus found with signs of beating is presumed born alive, thus qasamah and diyah are wajib.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi ruling on fetal homicide where a fully formed fetus is found dead with marks of violence. The commentator presents the standard ruling, then critiques its phrasing. We will unpack the legal reasoning behind the presumption of life and the debate on using the apparent state (zahir) for entitlement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
State the Hanafi position: if a fully formed fetus is found with a mark of beating, qasamah and diyah are obligatory; if no mark, nothing is due. Note the conditions: full formation and presence of a violent mark.
The mark must indicate homicide, not just any bruise.
2
Understand Ibn Nujaym's critique of the wording
The author points out three weaknesses: (1) a fetus is defined as being in the womb, so finding it alone is problematic; (2) mentioning both 'fetus' and 'stillborn' is redundant; (3) negating only 'a mark of beating' is insufficient; it should be 'a mark of killing' (wounding or strangulation). He suggests rephrasing.
This critique does not change the ruling itself, only its expression.
3
Analyze the debate on the use of zahir for entitlement
The passage discusses whether the apparent state (zahir) can be used to prove entitlement (istihqaq). For limbs, zahir is not sufficient; a just settlement is given. But for a fetus, which combines aspects of soul and limb, if fully formed and with marks, the presumption of life at birth justifies full diyah.
The argument from al-'Inayah questions this distinction, but the majority maintains the ruling.
4
Distinguish between treatment of limbs and the fetus
Limbs are treated as property; the fetus has a dual nature. When fully formed, the aspect of the soul predominates, so the presumption of life is stronger. This explains why zahir suffices for the fetus but not for limbs.
This distinction is key to understanding the Hanafi position.
5
Apply the ruling to a novel scenario
Consider a case where a fully formed fetus is found without any external marks but with internal injury. According to the rule, since no mark is visible, nothing is due unless other evidence of homicide exists. This shows the strict reliance on external signs.
The ruling emphasizes the need for clear evidence of violence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح ولو وجد فيهم جنين أو سقط ليس به أثر الضرب لا شيء على أهل المحلة لأنه لا يفوق الكبير حالاً، وإن كان به أثر الضرب وهو تام الخلق وجبت القسامة والدية عليهم لأن الظاهر أن تام الخلف ينفصل حياً إلى آخره. أقول: في تحرير هذه المسألة فتور من وجوه الأول أن الجنين على ما صرحوا به في عامة كتب اللغة الولد ما دام أنه في البطن فكيف يتصور يوجد فيهم جنين وحده وهو في بطن أمه، وأما وجوده مع أمه بمعزل عما نحن فيه لكون الحكم هناك للأم دون الجنين. والثاني أن ذكر الجنين يغني عن ذكر السقط لأن السقط على ما صرح به في كتب اللغة الولد الذي يسقط قبل تمامه والجنين يعم تام الخلق وغير تامة والثالث أن قوله ليس به أثر الضرب غير كاف في جواب المسألة إذ لا بد فيه من أن يكون به أثر الجراحة والخنق كما تقرر فيما سبق، فلاقتصار هنا على نفي أثر الضرب تقصير والأظهر أن يقال : ولو وجد فيهم ولد صغير ساقط ليس فيه أثر القتل فلا شيء عليهم فتدبر. قوله وإن كان به أثر الضرب وهو تام الخلق وجبت القسامة والدية عليهم لأن الظاهر أن تام الخلق ينفصل حياً. فإن قيل : الظاهر يصلح للدفع دون الاستحقاق ولهذا قلنا في عين الصبي ولسانه وذكره إذا لم يعلم صحته حكومة عدل عندنا وإن كان الظاهر سلامتها . أجيب بأنه إنما لم يجب في الأطراف قبل أن يعلم صحتها ما يجب في السليمة لأن الأطراف يسلك بها مسلك الأموال وليس تعظيم كتعظيم النفس فلم يجب فيها قبل العلم بالصحة قصاص أو دية بخلاف الجنين فإنه نفس من وجه عضو من وجه، فإذا انفصل تام الخلق وبه أثر الضرب وجب فيه القسامة والدية تعظيماً للنفوس لأن الظاهر أنه قتيل لوجود دلالة القتل وهو الأثر إذا الظاهر هو حال تام الخلق أن ينفصل حياً، وأما إذا وجد ميتاً ولا ثر به لا يجب فيه شيء فكذا هذا قال جمهور الشراح : ورد صاحب العناية جوابهم المزبور حيث قال بعد ذكر السؤال والجواب وهذا كما ترى مع تطويله لم يرد السؤال وربما قواه لأن الظاهر إذا لم يكن حجة للاستحقاق في الأموال وما سلك به مسلكها فلأن يكون فيما هو أعظم خطراً أولى انتهى. ولأن الجنين نفس فاعتبرنا جهة النفس إن انفصل حياً فيستدل عليه بتمام الخلق وعضو من وجه فاعتبرنا جهة العضو إن انفصل ميتاً فيستدل عليه بنقصان الخلق .
The commentator said: And if a fetus or stillborn is found among them without a mark of beating, nothing is upon the people of the locality, because it does not have the status of an adult immediately. But if there is a mark of beating and it is fully created, qasamah and diyah are obligatory upon them, because the apparent state is that a fully created being separates alive... I say: In the editing of this issue there is weakness in several respects: First, the fetus, as they stated in most language books, is the child as long as it is in the womb, so how can it be imagined that a fetus alone is found among them while it is in its mother's womb? As for its existence with its mother, that is separate from our discussion because the ruling there is for the mother, not the fetus. Second, mentioning the fetus suffices over mentioning the stillborn, because the stillborn, as stated in language books, is the child that falls before completion, and the fetus includes both complete and incomplete creation. Third, his saying 'without a mark of beating' is insufficient in answering the issue, because there must be a mark of wounding or strangulation as established previously. So limiting it to negating the mark of beating is a shortcoming. The more apparent is to say: And if a small fallen child is found among them without a mark of killing, nothing is upon them, so ponder. His saying: 'And if there is a mark of beating and it is fully created, qasamah and diyah are obligatory upon them, because the apparent state is that a fully created being separates alive.' If it is said: The apparent state is sufficient for defense, not for entitlement, and for this reason we said regarding the eye, tongue, and penis of a child, when its soundness is not known, there is a just settlement in our view, even though the apparent state is its soundness. The answer is: That which is not obligatory in limbs before knowing their soundness is what is obligatory for a sound limb, because limbs are treated like property and do not have the same veneration as the soul. So before knowing soundness, retaliation or diyah is not obligatory for them, unlike the fetus, which is a soul in one respect and a limb in another. So if it separates fully created and with a mark of beating, qasamah and diyah become obligatory for it, glorifying souls, because the apparent state is that it was slain, due to the presence of evidence of killing, which is the mark. Since the apparent state of a fully created being is that it separates alive. But if it is found dead without a trace, nothing is obligatory. So this is the same. The majority of commentators said: The author of al-'Inayah refuted their aforementioned answer, for he said after mentioning the question and answer: This, as you see, with its length, did not refute the question and possibly strengthened it, because if the apparent state is not a proof for entitlement in wealth and what is treated like it, then it is even more so in what is more serious. End quote. And because the fetus is a soul, we consider the aspect of soul if it separates alive, so it is inferred by complete creation; and it is a limb in one respect, so we consider the aspect of limb if it separates dead, so it is inferred by deficient creation.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage deals with the scenario where a fetus or stillborn is found in a locality. The commentator first states the ruling: if there is no mark of beating on the fetus, nothing is due; if there is a mark and the fetus is fully formed, qasamah and diyah are due because the presumption is that a fully formed fetus is born alive. The author (Ibn Nujaym) then critiques this ruling on three grounds: (1) A fetus is defined as being in the womb, so finding it separately is problematic; (2) mentioning both 'fetus' and 'stillborn' is redundant; (3) negating only 'a mark of beating' is insufficient – there must be a sign of wounding or strangulation. He suggests the clearer formulation: if a small fallen child is found without a mark of killing, nothing is due. The passage then engages in a debate about whether the apparent state (zahir) can be used to prove entitlement (istihqaq). The Hanafi position distinguishes between human life (nafs) and limbs (atraf): for limbs, the apparent soundness is not sufficient for claiming diyah, but for a fetus, which has a dual nature (soul and limb), if it is fully formed and has marks of violence, the presumption of life at birth justifies qasamah and diyah. The author of al-'Inayah (a commentator) argued that if the apparent state is not sufficient for financial claims, it should be even less so for serious matters like blood, but the majority maintain the ruling. The reasoning is that for a fully formed fetus that shows signs of beating, the presumption is that it was born alive and then killed; thus, qasamah and diyah apply. If there are no marks, it is presumed stillborn.

Key Terms
ظاهر (ẓāhir): The apparent or presumptive state; used in legal reasoning to establish a claim unless contrary evidence exists.
حكومة عدل (ḥukūmat ‘adl): A just assessment of compensation for injuries where the exact diyah is not prescribed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The fetus is treated like a limb, and any injury requires compensation.
D) The people of the locality are automatically responsible for any death in their area.

Key Takeaway
A fully formed fetus found dead with a mark of killing triggers qasamah and diyah due to the presumption of live birth and the veneration of human life.

Liability for a Corpse Found on a Riding Beast: Handlers vs. Owner
Ruling on diyah payment when a corpse is found on a riding animal with attendants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who bears diyah when a corpse is found on a riding animal with handlers, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the driver's liability.

Core Idea
Liability follows control (yad) – the handlers of the beast are liable, not the owner or locality.

This lesson examines a scenario from the chapter of diyat: a dead body is discovered on a riding animal accompanied by a driver, leader, or rider. The Hanafi school assigns responsibility to these attendants, not to the owner or the local community, based on the principle of effective control (yad). However, Imam Abu Yusuf introduces a nuance regarding the driver's intention, limiting his liability to secretive driving.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties involved
List the three types of handlers: driver (sā’iq), leader (qā’id), and rider (rākib). Understand their relationship to the beast.
The driver drives from behind, the leader leads from the front, the rider sits on the beast.
2
Understand the foundational analogy
The text compares the beast to a house: finding a corpse in a house makes the occupant liable due to control (yad). Similarly, the handlers have control over the beast.
Control is the operative cause (illa).
3
Analyze the dispute: Abu Yusuf's condition
Abu Yusuf holds that the driver is only liable if he was driving secretly (mukhtafiyan). If openly, he might be transporting a relative for burial.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
4
Apply to cases without handlers
If no driver, leader, or rider is present, liability falls on the people of the locality where the corpse is found.
The beast's location becomes the controlling factor.
5
Contrast with the owner-liability view
Some scholars analogize to a house and hold the owner liable. The Hanafi response: management (tadbir) of the beast belongs to the handlers, not necessarily the owner.
Ownership does not imply control in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : قتيل على دابة ومعها سائق أو قائد أو راكب فديته على عاقلته) دون أهل المحلة لأنه في يده فصار كما إذا كان في داره وإن اجتمع فيها السائق والقائد والراكب كانت الدية عليهم جميعاً لأن القتيل في أيديهم دون أهل المحلة فصار كما إذا وجد في دراهم، ولا يشترط أن يكونوا مالكين للدابة بخلاف الدار والفرق أن تدبير الدابة إليهم وإن لم يكونوا مالكين لها وتدبير الدار إلى مالكها وإن لم يكن ساكناً فيها. وقيل: القسامة والدية على مالك الدابة فعلى هذا أن لا فرق بينها وبين الدار وعن أبي يوسف أنه لا يجب على السائق إلا إذا كان يسوقها مختفياً لأن الإنسان قد ينقل قريبه الميت من مكان إلى مكان للدفن، وأما إذا كان على وجه الخفية فالظاهر أنه هو الذي قتله وإن لم يكن مع الدابة أحد فالدية والقسامة على أهل المحلة الذين وجد فيهم القتيل على الدابة لأن وجوده وحده على الدابة كوجوده في الموضع الذي فيه الدابة. وفي شرح الطحاوي أو كان الرجل يحمله على
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'A slain person on a riding beast, and with it a driver, leader, or rider, his diyah is upon his 'aqilah' – not the people of the locality, because he is in their control, so it is as if he is in their house. And if the driver, leader, and rider gather together, the diyah is upon all of them, because the slain person is in their control, not the people of the locality, so it becomes as if found in their money. And it is not a condition that they be owners of the beast, unlike the house. The difference is that the management of the beast belongs to them even if they do not own it, while the management of a house belongs to its owner even if he does not reside in it. And it is said: The qasamah and diyah are upon the owner of the beast, so on this there is no difference between it and a house. And from Abu Yusuf: It is not obligatory upon the driver unless he was driving it secretly, because a person may transport his deceased relative for burial. But if he does so secretly, then the apparent state is that he killed him. And if there is no one with the beast, then the diyah and qasamah are upon the people of the locality where the slain person is found on the beast, because his being alone on the beast is like his being in the place where the beast is. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: Or if a man carries him on...
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage deals with the scenario where a dead body is found on a riding animal (dabbah) and there are persons in charge: a driver (saa'iq), a leader (qa'id), or a rider (rakib). According to the Hanafi school, the diyah is on the 'aqilah of these persons, not on the people of the locality where the body is found. The reasoning is that the deceased is under their control (yad), analogous to being found in their house. If multiple persons are in charge, the diyah is divided among them. Notably, ownership of the animal is not required, unlike a house, because the management (tadbir) of the animal belongs to those handling it. There is also a view that the owner of the beast is liable. Abu Yusuf distinguishes the driver: he is liable only if he was driving secretly, as that indicates he might be the killer; otherwise, he could be transporting a relative for burial. If no one is with the animal, the liability falls on the people of the locality where the body is found. The passage from Sharh al-Tahawi appears to be cut off but likely continues with additional scenarios.

Key Terms
سائق (sā’iq): A person who drives the animal from behind.
قائد (qā’id): A person who leads the animal from the front.
راكب (rākib): A rider on the beast.
عاقلة (‘āqilah): The group of paternal male relatives responsible for paying diyah on behalf of a killer.
تدبير (tadbir): Management or control of the beast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Solely on the owner of the beast.
B) On all three (driver, leader, rider) collectively.
C) Only on the driver.
D) On the people of the locality where the beast is found.

Key Takeaway
The diyah for a corpse found on a riding animal is on the handlers' 'aqilah, because they have effective control (yad), even if they do not own the animal.

Ruling on a Fully Formed Fetus Found with Signs of Violence
Commentary defending obligation of qasamah and diyah for fully formed fetus with signs of violence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a fully formed fetus found with a mark of beating and explain the distinction between presumption for life versus property.

Core Idea
A fully formed fetus with a mark of beating triggers qasamah and diyah because the presumption of live birth makes the mark evidence of killing.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defends the Hanafi position that qasamah and diyah are obligatory for a fully formed fetus found with signs of violence. The author addresses an objection from Sahib al-'Inayah that presumption (al-zahir) cannot establish entitlement. The key distinction lies in the differing dignity of life compared to property: for limbs (treated as property), presumption alone is insufficient, but for a fetus (which has an aspect of life), the presumption of live birth elevates the mark into evidence of murder.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a fully formed fetus (tamm al-khalq) found with a mark of beating (athar al-darb). The ruling according to the matn: qasamah (collective oath) and diyah (blood money) are wajib upon those responsible.
The ruling applies only when the fetus is fully formed; if incomplete, different rules apply.
2
Follow the proof structure
The proof is that the apparent state (al-zahir) of a fully formed fetus is that it separates alive. Therefore, the mark of beating indicates the fetus was killed after live birth. This is a rational analogy (qiyas) based on the dignity of life: the fetus is a life in one respect, so indications of killing are taken seriously.
The proof relies on the principle 'al-zahir yufid al-zann' – the apparent state creates a strong presumption (ghalabat al-zann).
3
Analyse the objection and response
The objection: presumption can repel liability but not establish entitlement; for example, a minor's eye, tongue, or penis only warrants equitable assessment (hukumat 'adl) even if soundness is presumed. Response: Limbs are treated as property – their dignity is lesser – so full compensation (qisas/diyah) requires certain knowledge. The fetus, however, is a life in one respect – its dignity is greater. Thus, the presumption of live birth, combined with the mark, elevates the case to murder, justifying qasamah and diyah.
The author of al-'Inayah criticized this response, arguing that if presumption fails for property, it fails even more for life. The commentator defends the matn, reaffirming the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وإن كان به أثر الضرب وهو تام الخلق وجبت القسامة والدية عليهم لأن الظاهر أن تام الخلق ينفصل حياً. فإن قيل : الظاهر يصلح للدفع دون الاستحقاق ولهذا قلنا في عين الصبي ولسانه وذكره إذا لم يعلم صحته حكومة عدل عندنا وإن كان الظاهر سلامتها . أجيب بأنه إنما لم يجب في الأطراف قبل أن يعلم صحتها ما يجب في السليمة لأن الأطراف يسلك بها مسلك الأموال وليس تعظيم كتعظيم النفس فلم يجب فيها قبل العلم بالصحة قصاص أو دية بخلاف الجنين فإنه نفس من وجه عضو من وجه، فإذا انفصل تام الخلق وبه أثر الضرب وجب فيه القسامة والدية تعظيماً للنفوس لأن الظاهر أنه قتيل لوجود دلالة القتل وهو الأثر إذا الظاهر هو حال تام الخلق أن ينفصل حياً، وأما إذا وجد ميتاً ولا ثر به لا يجب فيه شيء فكذا هذا قال جمهور الشراح : ورد صاحب العناية جوابهم المزبور حيث قال بعد ذكر السؤال والجواب وهذا كما ترى مع تطويله لم يرد السؤال وربما قواه لأن الظاهر إذا لم يكن حجة للاستحقاق في الأموال وما سلك به مسلكها فلأن يكون فيما هو أعظم خطراً أولى انتهى. ولأن الجنين نفس فاعتبرنا جهة النفس إن انفصل حياً فيستدل عليه بتمام الخلق وعضو من وجه فاعتبرنا جهة العضو إن انفصل ميتاً فيستدل عليه بنقصان الخلق.
His statement: 'And if there is a mark of beating on it and it is fully formed, then qasamah and blood-money are obligatory upon them because the apparent state is that the fully-formed one separates alive.' So if it is said: The apparent state is valid for repelling, not for entitlement; therefore we said concerning the eye of a minor, his tongue, and his penis, if their soundness is not known, there is an equitable assessment with us, even if the apparent state is their soundness. The answer: It is that it was not obligatory in the limbs before knowing their soundness what is obligatory in the sound ones because limbs are treated in the way of property and their dignity is not like the dignity of life, so before knowledge of soundness, retaliation or blood-money is not obligatory, unlike the fetus, for it is a life in one respect and a limb in another. So when it separates fully formed and has a mark of beating, qasamah and blood-money become obligatory as a glorification of lives, because the apparent state is that it is killed due to the presence of an indication of killing which is the mark, since the apparent state of the fully formed is that it separates alive. As for when it is found dead without a mark, nothing is obligatory. And so this is. The majority of commentators said: The author of al-`Inayah rejected their aforementioned answer, stating after mentioning the question and answer: 'And this, as you see, with its length, does not refute the question, and perhaps it strengthens it because if the apparent state is not a proof for entitlement in property and what is treated like it, then it is even more fitting that it not be so in what is of greater gravity.' End quote. And because the fetus is a life, we consider the aspect of life if it separates alive, so it is inferred by completeness of creation; and it is a limb in one respect, so we consider the aspect of limb if it separates dead, so it is inferred by incompleteness of creation.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement regarding a fully formed fetus found with signs of violence. The commentator begins by quoting the matn: if a fully formed fetus is found with a mark of beating, then qasamah and blood-money (diyah) are obligatory upon those responsible. The proof is the apparent presumption: a fully formed fetus normally separates alive. The commentator then addresses an objection: the presumption (al-zahir) is only sufficient for repelling liability, not for establishing entitlement, as we see in cases of a child's eye, tongue, or penis where we only award equitable assessment (hukumat 'adl) even though the presumption is soundness. The answer distinguishes limbs from life: limbs are treated like property and have lesser dignity, so before confirming injury we do not impose full diyah or qisas. But the fetus is a life in one respect and a limb in another. When fully formed with a mark of beating, the aspect of life dominates, and since the presumption is that it was born alive, the mark indicates it was killed, thus qasamah and diyah are due. The commentator then reports that the majority of commentators mention that the author of al-'Inayah criticized this answer, arguing that if the presumption is not sufficient for entitlement in property cases, it is even less sufficient in matters of greater gravity. Finally, the commentator reaffirms the Hanafi position: the fetus is a life, so if it separates alive we look to full creation; if dead, we look to incomplete creation. The passage thus defends the Hanafi ruling against objections.

Key Terms
تام الخلق (tamm al-khalq): A fetus that is fully formed and developed, which according to the Hanafi school is presumed to have been born alive.
القسامة (al-qasamah): A collective oath of fifty men from the accused party that they did not kill the victim, required when a corpse is found with signs of violence.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money compensation paid by the 'aqilah (tribe) of the killer, or in qasamah cases by the responsible party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the Hanafi school require qasamah and diyah for a fully formed fetus found with a mark of beating?
A) Because the fetus is always considered a human being with full rights.
B) Because the fully formed state creates a presumption of live birth, making the mark evidence of killing.
C) Because the mark alone is sufficient proof of murder.
D) Because the fetus is part of the mother and thus her injuries extend.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school imposes qasamah and diyah on the responsible party for a fully formed fetus found with a mark of beating, due to the presumption of life and the dignity of the human soul.

Corpse on an Animal: Liability of Handlers
Matn and commentary on qasamah liability for a corpse on an animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on diyah when a corpse is found on an animal with handlers and apply the principle of possession (yad) to determine liability.

Core Idea
Liability for a corpse on an animal rests on the handler in possession, not the owner.

In Hanafi criminal law (diyat), when a corpse is discovered on a riding animal, the key question is who must pay blood-money (diyah). The matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq and its commentary clarify that liability attaches to the person(s) in physical control (yad) of the animal, not necessarily its owner. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its rational basis, and the intra-Hanafi differences, including Abu Yusuf's special condition for stealthy driving.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic case and ruling
When a dead body is found on an animal accompanied by a driver, leader, or rider, the diyah is upon the 'aqilah (tribe) of those handlers, not the neighborhood. This is because the body is in their possession (yad), analogous to being in a house.
The handlers' liability arises from direct control over the animal.
2
Understand the role of possession (yad) vs ownership
The commentary emphasizes that management (tadbir) of the animal is with the handlers even if they are not owners. This differs from a house, where control rests with the owner regardless of presence. Hence ownership is not a condition for liability.
If the animal has no handlers, liability falls on the neighborhood.
3
Analyze the differences of opinion (ikhtilaf)
Abu Yusuf holds the driver liable only if driving stealthily (since normal driving could be to move a dead relative). Some scholars shift liability to the animal's owner if known, by analogy to a case from Kitab al-'Itaq. The majority (including Muhammad) maintain handler liability regardless of ownership.
The mufta bihi is handler liability per Muhammad and later preference.
4
Apply the ruling to scenarios with no handlers
If no one is with the animal, the diyah and qasamah are upon the people of the neighborhood where the animal is found. The corpse's presence on the animal alone is treated like its presence in that locality.
Sharh al-Tahawi extends the same to a person carrying the corpse on his back.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that liability is on the handler in possession, regardless of ownership. This is the zahir al-riwayah as reported by Muhammad and adopted by the majority of later scholars.
The commentator does not explicitly prefer, but the structure supports handler liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : قتيل على دابة ومعها سائق أو قائد أو راكب فديته على عاقلته) دون أهل المحلة لأنه في يده فصار كما إذا كان في داره وإن اجتمع فيها السائق والقائد والراكب كانت الدية عليهم جميعاً لأن القتيل في أيديهم دون أهل المحلة فصار كما إذا وجد في دراهم، ولا يشترط أن يكونوا مالكين للدابة بخلاف الدار والفرق أن تدبير الدابة إليهم وإن لم يكونوا مالكين لها وتدبير الدار إلى مالكها وإن لم يكن ساكناً فيها. وقيل: القسامة والدية على مالك الدابة فعلى هذا أن لا فرق بينها وبين الدار وعن أبي يوسف أنه لا يجب على السائق إلا إذا كان يسوقها مختفياً لأن الإنسان قد ينقل قريبه الميت من مكان إلى مكان للدفن، وأما إذا كان على وجه الخفية فالظاهر أنه هو الذي قتله وإن لم يكن مع الدابة أحد فالدية والقسامة على أهل المحلة الذين وجد فيهم القتيل على الدابة لأن وجوده وحده على الدابة كوجوده في الموضع الذي فيه الدابة. وفي شرح الطحاوي أو كان الرجل يحمله على ظهره فهو كالذي مع الدابة وظاهر عبارة المؤلف أنه لا فرق بين أن يكون المالك معروفاً أو لا وفي شرح الطحاوي: فالقسامة والدية عليهم هكذا ذكر محمد ، ولم يفصل بين ما إذا كان للدابة مالك وبين إذا لم يكن بل أطلق الجواب ومن مشايخنا من قال : هذا إذا لم يكن للدابة مالك معروف وإنما يعرف ذلك القائد والسائق، فأما إذا كان مالك الدابة معروفاً فإنما تجب القسامة والدية على مالك الدابة نظير هذا ما قال محمد في كتاب العتاق : إن الرجل إذ استولد جارية في يده ثم أقر أنها لفلان إن كان المقر له مالكاً معروفاً لهذه الجارية صدق المستولد ولم تصر أم ولده، وإن لم يكن المقر له مالكاً معروفاً لم يصدق لأنها صارت أم ولد له من حيث الظاهر فكذلك هنا. ومن المشايخ من قال : سواء كان للدابة مالك معروف أو لم يكن فإن القسامة تجب على الذي في يده الدابة والدية على عاقلته ولو وقعت المنازعة بين أهل المحلة وبين السائق كان القول قول السائق أن الدابة دابته . يسمع منهم
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'A killed person on an animal, and with it a driver, or a leader, or a rider, then his blood-money is upon his 'aqilah' not upon the people of the neighborhood because he is in his possession, so it becomes as if he were in his house. And if the driver, leader, and rider are all present together, the blood-money is upon all of them because the killed person is in their possession, not the people of the neighborhood, so it becomes as if found in their hands. And it is not a condition that they be owners of the animal, unlike the house; and the difference is that the management of the animal is upon them even if they are not owners, while the management of the house is upon its owner even if he is not residing in it. And it is said: the qasamah and blood-money are upon the owner of the animal; thus on this there is no difference between it and the house. And from Abu Yusuf: it is not obligatory upon the driver unless he is driving it stealthily, because a person may move his dead relative from one place to another for burial, but if it is stealthily, then the apparent is that he killed him. And if there is no one with the animal, then the blood-money and qasamah are upon the people of the neighborhood in which the killed person on the animal is found, because his presence alone on the animal is like his presence in the place where the animal is. In Sharh al-Tahawi: or if a man carries him on his back, he is like the one with the animal. And the apparent wording of the author is that there is no difference between whether the owner is known or not. In Sharh al-Tahawi: so the qasamah and blood-money are upon them; thus Muhammad mentioned, and he did not differentiate between when the animal has an owner and when it does not, but rather gave an absolute answer. And some of our scholars said: this is when the animal has no known owner, and only the leader and driver are known; as for when the animal's owner is known, then the qasamah and blood-money are upon the animal's owner. Analogous to this is what Muhammad said in Kitab al-'Itaq: that if a man makes a slave-girl in his hand an umm walad, then confesses that she belongs to so-and-so, if the confessee is a known owner of this slave-girl, the one who made her umm walad is believed and she does not become an umm walad; but if the confessee is not a known owner, he is not believed because she has become an umm walad in apparent terms. So here likewise. And some of the scholars said: whether the animal has a known owner or not, the qasamah is obligatory upon the one in whose hand the animal is, and the blood-money upon his 'aqilah. And if a dispute occurs between the people of the neighborhood and the driver, the statement of the driver that: 'the animal is his animal' is accepted.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling from the matn: when a dead body is found on a riding animal accompanied by a driver, leader, or rider, the blood-money (diyah) is upon the 'aqilah (responsible tribe) of those handlers, not upon the people of the neighborhood. The commentary explains the reasoning: the dead body is in their possession (yad) as they have direct control over the animal, similar to a house. When multiple handlers are present, they all share liability. Notably, ownership of the animal is not a condition; the key is control (tadbir). The passage then reports several differences of opinion. Some scholars place liability on the owner of the animal. Abu Yusuf restricts liability for the driver to cases where he drives stealthily, since a normal driver might be transporting a dead relative. If no handlers are present, liability falls on the neighborhood. The commentary from Sharh al-Tahawi extends the ruling to a person carrying the corpse on his back. The Hanafi school has ikhtilaf on whether the animal's owner being known changes the ruling. Some say if the owner is known, the owner is liable (by analogy to a case in Kitab al-'Itaq). Others say the handler remains liable regardless. The passage also mentions that in a dispute, the driver's claim that the animal is his is accepted against the neighborhood. Overall, the lesson clarifies the criteria for liability in qasamah cases involving animals.

Key Terms
السائق (al-sa'iq): The driver who drives the animal from behind.
القائد (al-qa'id): The leader who holds the reins and guides the animal.
العاقلة (al-'aqilah): The agnatic male relatives (tribe) who are responsible for paying blood-money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in a qasamah case where a corpse is found on an animal with a known owner, who is primarily liable?
A) The owner of the animal
B) The handlers (driver/leader/rider) regardless of ownership
C) The people of the neighborhood
D) The state (Bayt al-Mal)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, liability for a corpse on an animal is on the handlers (based on possession), not the owner, unless the handlers are absent, then on the neighborhood.
